Second Objection from Another Copy and Its Answer
The commentator presents a version from another copy and defends the causal role of the attribute's essence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator defends the causal role of an attribute's essence over its subsistence, and how he resolves the charge of circularity in the second objection.

Core Idea
The attribute's essence, not its subsistence, is the true cause—thus avoiding circularity.

This passage continues the discussion on causality in kalam. A second objection arises from a variant reading of al-Arba‘īn, charging that if the attribute's subsistence depends on spatialization, a circularity results. The commentator clarifies that the cause is the attribute's essence existing in itself, not its subsistence, thereby blocking the circularity. We will trace his reasoning and the subsequent debate about the nature of accidental existence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the opening lines where the objection from another copy is presented. Note the claim that if subsistence depends on spatialization, circularity follows.
2
Understand the commentator's response
The commentator distinguishes between the attribute's essence (dhāt) and its subsistence (qiyām). He argues that the cause is the essence as it exists in itself, not the subsistence.
This is the key move to avoid circularity.
3
Examine the two interpretations of dependence
The passage discusses two possible meanings of 'dependence' (tawaqquf). The author chooses the first branch and denies that mutual necessitation constitutes impossible circularity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ربما قال : إشارة إلى ما وجد في نسخة أخرى من الأربعين هكذا قيام الصفة التي هي علة للحصول إن توقف على التحيز أي الحصول في الحيز لزم الدور لأنه لما عللنا حصول الجوهر في حيزه بتلك الصفة القائمة به كان الحصول متوقفاً على قيامها به، والمفروض أن قيامها به متوقف على ذلك الحصول وهو الدور يرد عليه ما مر من أنَّ العلة ذات الصفة من حيث وجودها في نفسها، ولا يلزم من هذا توقف الحصول على قيامها بالجوهر وإلا أي وإن لم يتوقف قيام الصفة على الحصول في الحيز جاز قوله : (قد تكون ذات الصفة أي قام تلك الصفة كما يدل عليه قوله : لا قيامها أي ليس أن المراد نفس الصفة مع قطع النظر عن القيام وهذا مبني على ما ذكره الشارح فيما سبق من وجود العرض مقدم بالذات على قيامه بالموضوع بدليل قولهم : وجد السواد فقام بالجسم فيجوز أن تكون الصفة الموجودة في حال قيامها علة للحصول، وإن لم يكن للقيام مدخل في العلية وأما إذا كان وجود العرض في نفسه هو وجوده في الموضوع، فلا يتمشى هذا الجواب لأنَّ وجود تلك الصفة مما يتوقف عليها وجود قيامها بالمحل، فيكون حصول الجوهر في الحيز لأن التحيز التبعي موقوف على التحيز الأصلي فيلزم الدور . قوله : ( ولا يلزم إلخ) وإن كان في حال القيام .. قوله : ( وإن لم يتوقف كان الظاهر من الشق الثاني أنَّ المراد من التوقف جواز الانفكاك ليصح الملازمة، اكتفى المصنف بذلك واختار الشق الأول ومنع بطلان التالي لأن اللزوم صدور الدور بمعنى استلزام كل منهما للآخر، وليس بممتنع وأما صاحب لباب الأربعين فقد أورد قوله : ( لأنه قد تكون ذات الصفة علة للحصول ) فيه بحث لأنه إن أريد أن ذات الصفة قبل قيامها بالموصوف علة له فلا وجه له لأنها معدومة حينئذ، ولو سلم وجودها فاقتضاؤها لحصول هذا الموصوف في المكان دون آخر ترجیح بلا مرجح وإن أريد أنها مع قيامها به علة له وهو الحق إذ الظاهر أن علة الكون للكائنة مشروط بقيامه بالكائن، فقد جاء الدور واحتيج إلى الجواب الآخر. انفكاك العلة التي هي تلك الصفة القائمة بالجوهر عن المعلول الذي هو الحصول في الحيز، لأنه لما لم يتوقف قيامها به على الحصول أمكن القيام بدون الحصول، فأمكن أن توجد تلك الصفة قائمة بالجوهر خالية عن معلولها الذي هو الحصول
His statement: 'Perhaps it is said' refers to what is found in another copy of al-Arba‘īn, thus: 'The subsistence of the attribute which is the cause of the occurrence: if it depends on spatialization, i.e., occurrence in the locus, then circularity follows.' That is because when we caused the occurrence of the substance in its locus by that attribute subsisting in it, the occurrence depends on its subsistence in it, and it is assumed that its subsistence in it depends on that occurrence, and that is circularity. It is responded to by what has passed, that the cause is the essence of the attribute in terms of its existence in itself, and it does not follow from this that the occurrence depends on its subsistence in the substance. Otherwise, i.e., if the subsistence of the attribute does not depend on the occurrence in the locus, then it is permissible. His statement: 'It may be that the essence of the attribute' meaning that the attribute subsists, as indicated by his statement: 'not its subsistence,' i.e., it is not that the intended meaning is the attribute itself disregarding subsistence. This is based on what the commentator mentioned earlier, that the existence of an accident is prior in essence to its subsistence in the subject, as evidenced by their saying: 'The blackness existed, then it subsisted in the body.' So it is permissible that the attribute existing in the state of its subsistence be the cause of the occurrence, even if subsistence has no role in causality. But if the existence of the accident in itself is its existence in the subject, then this answer does not apply, because the existence of that attribute is something on which the existence of its subsistence in the locus depends, so the occurrence of the substance in the locus—because derivative spatialization depends on original spatialization—entails circularity. His statement: 'And it does not follow etc.' even if it is in the state of subsistence. His statement: 'And if it does not depend, it is apparent from the second branch that the intended meaning of dependence is the possibility of separation to make the implication valid; the author sufficed with that and chose the first branch and denied the invalidity of the consequent, because the necessity is the issuance of circularity in the sense of each necessitating the other, and it is not impossible. As for the author of Lubāb al-Arba‘īn, he objected: 'Because it may be that the essence of the attribute is the cause of the occurrence.' There is a discussion: if by that is meant that the essence of the attribute before its subsistence in the qualified is the cause, then it has no basis because it would be non-existent then; and even if its existence is conceded, its demanding the occurrence of this qualified in this place rather than another would be a preference without a preferrer. And if by that is meant that it together with its subsistence in it is the cause—which is the truth—then it is apparent that the cause of spatialization for the spatial thing is conditional upon its subsistence in the spatial thing, so circularity arises and another answer is needed. The separation of the cause which is that attribute subsisting in the substance from the effect which is the occurrence in the locus: because when its subsistence in it does not depend on the occurrence, subsistence is possible without the occurrence, so it is possible that that attribute exists subsisting in the substance devoid of its effect which is the occurrence.
Source pages p. 171

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection and its answer. It begins with a quotation from another version of al-Arba‘īn: if the subsistence of the attribute depends on spatialization (taḥayyuz), circularity arises. The commentator responds by reiterating that the cause is the attribute's essence (dhāt al-ṣifa), not its subsistence, so the occurrence does not depend on subsistence. If subsistence does not depend on occurrence, then it is possible for the cause to exist without its effect. Then there is a long explanatory discussion about the meaning of 'essence of the attribute' and the relationship between an accident's existence and its subsistence. The commentator notes that if the accident's existence is considered prior to its subsistence, then the essence can be the cause even without subsistence's role. But if the accident's existence is identical to its subsistence, then circularity cannot be avoided. The commentator then discusses the two possible meanings of 'dependence' (tawaqquf): either necessary posteriority or the impossibility of existence without the depended-upon thing. The author chooses the first branch and denies the invalidity of the consequent, arguing that mutual necessitation does not constitute impossible circularity. Then a contrary opinion from the author of Lubāb al-Arba‘īn is cited, who argues that if the essence alone is considered, it is non-existent in itself before subsistence, and if considered with subsistence, then circularity returns. The passage concludes by stating the alternative: if subsistence does not depend on occurrence, then the cause (the attribute subsisting) can be separate from its effect (the occurrence).

Key Terms
قيام
تحيز
دور
ذات الصفة
عرض
توقف
انفكاك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the attribute's subsistence must be the cause.
Correction: The commentator clarifies that the essence of the attribute can be the cause, independent of its subsistence.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, in what sense can the essence of an attribute (dhāt al-ṣifa) be the cause of the substance's occurrence in a place?
A) It is non-existent before subsistence.
B) It exists prior to its subsistence in the substance.
C) It exists only when subsisting.
D) It is a universal essence.

Key Takeaway
The commentator resolves the circularity by distinguishing the attribute's essence from its subsistence, allowing the essence to be the cause independently.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your life, do you often attribute outcomes to the 'subsistence' of means—like your effort, intelligence, or circumstances—rather than to the essence of Allah's power? How does distinguishing essence from subsistence challenge you to shift your reliance from means to the Creator?
?
The passage shows that circular reasoning can be avoided by focusing on the essence. Are there beliefs or practices that have become circular in your faith journey, where you rely on the 'subsistence' of actions rather than the essence of your intention? What would it mean to break that cycle?

The Claim that Tawaqquf Does Not Entail Circularity
Exposition of a claim about tawaqquf and the author's initial reply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial claim that dependence as impossibility of separation does not entail impossible circularity, and the author's response that the claim misses the Imam's point.

Core Idea
Dependence without circularity is possible if cause and effect are mutually entailing.

We begin with a potential objection to al-Razi's argument: if tawaqquf means inseparable concomitance, then circularity is not necessarily implied. The author responds that this objection is not directed at the Imam's actual words. This lesson sets up the debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note the two definitions of tawaqquf presented from al-Arba‘īn.
The passage starts with 'وقد يقال' and ends with 'يظهر ذلك عليك إذا تأملت'.
2
Identify the two senses
The passage distinguishes between tawaqquf as necessary posteriority and as impossibility of existence without the dependee. Understand how each relates to circularity.
The second sense allows mutual entailment without circularity.
3
Understand the author's response
The author (al-muṣannif) says the objection does not apply to Imam al-Razi's statement. Reflect on why the objector's interpretation might be missing the point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يقال : إِنَّ التوقف بمعنى عدم جواز الانفكاك لا يوجب دوراً ممتنعاً وتقريره على ما في كتاب الأربعين، أنه إن عني بالتوقف وجوب تأخر الموقوف عن الموقوف عليه لم يلزم من عدم التوقف إمكان حصول العلة بدون المعلول، وإن عني به عدم جواز وجوده بدون الموقوف عليه لم يلزم من التوقف بهذا المعنى الدور لجواز أن تكون العلة والمعلول متلازمين مع كون المعلول محتاجاً إلى علته بلا عكس قال المصنف : (وهو) أي ما ذكره هذا القائل غير وارد على كلام الإمام يظهر ذلك عليك إذا تأملت وقد وجهه بعض تلامذته بأن مجرد امتناع الانفكاك من الجانبين، وإن لم يستلزم دوراً ممتنعاً، إلا أن هاهنا أمراً آخر يستلزمه، إذ قد صرح الإمام بأنَّ قيام الصفة بالشيء معناه أن تحيزها تابع لتحيزه، ولا شك أن تحيز الجوهر تابع لقيام الصفة لكونه علة له فيلزم الدور الممتنع وهذا مردود أما أولاً، فلأنه لا تصريح بذلك المعنى في هذه النسخة بل فيها أنَّ قيام الصفة المذكورة بالجوهر، إما أن يتوقف الاحتمالين وهي بطلان الملازمة على احتمال وبطلان التالي على احتمال آخر حسماً لمادة الشبهة . الأخرى قوله: (وهذا مردود إلخ يمكن أن يقال : مقصود الموجه أنَّ الترديد في معنى القيام فكأنه قيل في قيام الصفة إن توقف على التحيز بأنَّ فسر بالتبعية في التحيز لزم الدور وهو ظاهر، وإن لم يتوقف عليه بأنَّ فسر بالاختصاص الناعت بالجوهر من غير حصول في الحيز كما في الواجب تعالى فلا يكون القيام علة مستلزمة، وحينئذ يكون مآل النسختين واحداً إلا أنه ترك في النسختين احتمال عدم التوقف لكونه خلاف ما هو المشهور من معنى القيام، وذكر في استظهاراً، وحينئذ اندفع الرد بالوجوه الثلاثة، أما الأول : فلان التصريح غير لازم لكونه مشهوراً، واستفسار هذا القائل على معنى التوقف غير موجه لما عرفت من أنَّ المراد به توقف التأخر والترديد مبني على تفسير القيام وأما الثاني : فلانه لم يجعل التمسك بمعنى القيام خبر لدليل آخر أورده في التوقف بناء على تفسيري القيام فهو في الحقيقة تمسك بمعنى القيام كما في النسخة الأخرى وأما الثالث فلان ما ذكره من الوجه بيان المراد لأمام بحيث لا يرد عليه ما ذكره قوله: (وهو غير وارد إذا تأملت قيل : معناه أنَّ اعتراض الإمام غير وارد فلا يحتاج إلى الجواب المذكور، وذلك بناء على أن علة الكائنية ذات الصفة لا قيامها كما مر وأنت خبير بأنه تعسف ظاهر ومخالف لتوجيه تلميذه الذي هو أعلم بمراده
It may be said that dependence in the sense of the impossibility of separation does not necessitate impossible circularity. Its exposition according to what is in the book al-Arba‘īn is that if by 'dependence' is meant the necessity of the dependant being posterior to its dependee, then from the absence of dependence it does not follow that the cause can exist without the effect. And if by it is meant the impossibility of its existence without the dependee, then from dependence in this sense circularity does not follow, because it is possible that the cause and effect are mutually entailing while the effect is in need of its cause without the reverse. The author said: 'It' i.e., what this objector mentioned, is not directed at the words of the Imam; that will become clear to you if you reflect. One of his students explained it by saying: Mere mutual inseparability, even if it does not entail impossible circularity, yet here there is another matter that does entail it, for the Imam explicitly stated that the subsistence of an attribute in a thing means that its spatialization is dependent on its spatialization, and there is no doubt that the spatialization of the substance is dependent on the subsistence of the attribute because it is its cause, so impossible circularity follows. And this is refuted: firstly, because there is no explicit statement of that meaning in this copy; rather, in it, the subsistence of the aforementioned attribute in the substance is either dependent... the two possibilities are the invalidity of the implication on one possibility and the invalidity of the consequent on another possibility, decisively cutting off the source of the doubt. The other: His statement: 'And this is refuted, etc.' It can be said: The intended meaning of the interpreter is that the disjunction in the meaning of qiyām is such that it can be said about the subsistence of the attribute: if it depends on spatialization, i.e., interpreted as dependence in spatialization, then circularity follows—that is apparent. And if it does not depend on it, i.e., interpreted as attributive specification of the substance without occurrence in the locus, as in the case of the Necessary Exalted, then the subsistence is not a necessitating cause. Then the outcome of the two copies is the same, except that in the two copies the possibility of non-dependence was omitted because it is contrary to the well-known meaning of qiyām, and it was mentioned for support. Then the refutation is repelled by three points: First, because explicit statement is not necessary since it is well-known, and this objector's inquiry about the meaning of dependence is not valid because we have explained that what is meant by it is the dependence of posteriority, and the disjunction is based on the interpretation of qiyām. Second, because he did not make the adherence to the meaning of qiyām a report of another evidence he adduced in dependence based on the two interpretations of qiyām; it is in reality adherence to the meaning of qiyām as in the other copy. Third, because what he mentioned of the aspect is an explanation of the intention of the Imam so that what he mentioned does not apply to him. His statement: 'It is not directed' when said: 'If you reflect, it means that the objection of the Imam is not valid, so there is no need for the answer mentioned.' This is based on the fact that the cause of spatialization is the essence of the attribute, not its subsistence, as has passed. And you are aware that this is an apparent contortion and contradicts the interpretation of his student who is more knowledgeable of his intent.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is a discussion and refutation of a claim about the meaning of tawaqquf (dependence). It begins with the claim that if tawaqquf is understood as the impossibility of separation (i.e., inseparable concomitance), it does not entail impossible circularity. The exposition from al-Arba‘īn distinguishes two senses: (1) necessary posteriority, and (2) impossibility of existence without the dependee. In the second sense, circularity does not necessarily follow because cause and effect can be mutually entailing without the effect being dependent on the cause in the same way. The author (al-muṣannif) responds that this claim is not directed at Imam al-Razi's words. A student of al-Razi offered an interpretation: even if mutual inseparability does not entail circularity, there is another factor—the explicit statement of al-Razi that subsistence (qiyām) means derivative spatialization—which leads to circularity. The commentator then refutes this interpretation: first, this explicit statement is not present in the current copy; second, the student's interpretation misconstrues the disjunction in the meaning of qiyām. The passage concludes by noting that the correct view is that the cause is the attribute's essence, not its subsistence, and that the student's interpretation strains the text.

Key Terms
توقف
انفكاك
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if two things are mutually inseparable, they must be circular and hence impossible.
Correction: The passage shows that mutual inseparability does not necessarily imply circularity because the dependency can be one-way even if both exist together.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the second sense of tawaqquf?
A) Necessary posteriority
B) Impossibility of existence without the dependee
C) Inseparability of cause and effect
D) Mutual entailment

Key Takeaway
Tawaqquf as impossibility of separation does not automatically entail impossible circularity; the dependency relation must be carefully defined.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own life, do you treat any relationship or habit as though it has a mutual dependence with Allah, forgetting that your dependence on Him is one-way and total? Which specific area of your life needs this correction?
?
The objector's argument was dismissed because it missed the Imam's actual position. Are there theological objections you have entertained about Allah or His attributes without first understanding the precise definitions used by scholars? How can you guard against misdirected criticism?
?
Reflect on the idea that dependence does not imply circularity. In your worship, do you sometimes act as though your actions entangle Allah in a reciprocal obligation? Where do you need to reaffirm that you are solely dependent on Him, without reverse?

The Student's Interpretation and First Refutation
Presentation of the student's interpretation and the initial refutation regarding textual evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the student's introduction of the meaning of qiyām as leading to circularity, and the commentator's first refutation that this interpretation lacks textual support in the available copy.

Core Idea
The student's argument relies on an explicit statement that is not present in the source.

A student of al-Razi attempted to counter the objection by citing a definition of qiyām (subsistence) that would cause circularity. The commentator refutes this by pointing out that the claimed explicit statement is not found in the current version of al-Arba‘īn. This lesson examines that exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation of the student's interpretation and the first refutation.
The passage begins with 'وقد وجهه بعض تلامذته' and ends with 'حسماً لمادة الشبهة'.
2
Understand the student's argument
The student says that al-Razi explicitly stated that the subsistence of an attribute in a substance means its spatialization is dependent on the substance's spatialization, which would lead to circularity.
Note that the student uses this to show that even if mutual inseparability doesn't entail circularity, this additional factor does.
3
Identify the first refutation
The commentator says this explicit statement is not present in the current copy; rather, the copy contains a disjunction about two possibilities. So the student's argument is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يقال : إِنَّ التوقف بمعنى عدم جواز الانفكاك لا يوجب دوراً ممتنعاً وتقريره على ما في كتاب الأربعين، أنه إن عني بالتوقف وجوب تأخر الموقوف عن الموقوف عليه لم يلزم من عدم التوقف إمكان حصول العلة بدون المعلول، وإن عني به عدم جواز وجوده بدون الموقوف عليه لم يلزم من التوقف بهذا المعنى الدور لجواز أن تكون العلة والمعلول متلازمين مع كون المعلول محتاجاً إلى علته بلا عكس قال المصنف : (وهو) أي ما ذكره هذا القائل غير وارد على كلام الإمام يظهر ذلك عليك إذا تأملت وقد وجهه بعض تلامذته بأن مجرد امتناع الانفكاك من الجانبين، وإن لم يستلزم دوراً ممتنعاً، إلا أن هاهنا أمراً آخر يستلزمه، إذ قد صرح الإمام بأنَّ قيام الصفة بالشيء معناه أن تحيزها تابع لتحيزه، ولا شك أن تحيز الجوهر تابع لقيام الصفة لكونه علة له فيلزم الدور الممتنع وهذا مردود أما أولاً، فلأنه لا تصريح بذلك المعنى في هذه النسخة بل فيها أنَّ قيام الصفة المذكورة بالجوهر، إما أن يتوقف الاحتمالين وهي بطلان الملازمة على احتمال وبطلان التالي على احتمال آخر حسماً لمادة الشبهة . الأخرى قوله: (وهذا مردود إلخ يمكن أن يقال : مقصود الموجه أنَّ الترديد في معنى القيام فكأنه قيل في قيام الصفة إن توقف على التحيز بأنَّ فسر بالتبعية في التحيز لزم الدور وهو ظاهر، وإن لم يتوقف عليه بأنَّ فسر بالاختصاص الناعت بالجوهر من غير حصول في الحيز كما في الواجب تعالى فلا يكون القيام علة مستلزمة، وحينئذ يكون مآل النسختين واحداً إلا أنه ترك في النسختين احتمال عدم التوقف لكونه خلاف ما هو المشهور من معنى القيام، وذكر في استظهاراً، وحينئذ اندفع الرد بالوجوه الثلاثة، أما الأول : فلان التصريح غير لازم لكونه مشهوراً، واستفسار هذا القائل على معنى التوقف غير موجه لما عرفت من أنَّ المراد به توقف التأخر والترديد مبني على تفسير القيام وأما الثاني : فلانه لم يجعل التمسك بمعنى القيام خبر لدليل آخر أورده في التوقف بناء على تفسيري القيام فهو في الحقيقة تمسك بمعنى القيام كما في النسخة الأخرى وأما الثالث فلان ما ذكره من الوجه بيان المراد لأمام بحيث لا يرد عليه ما ذكره قوله: (وهو غير وارد إذا تأملت قيل : معناه أنَّ اعتراض الإمام غير وارد فلا يحتاج إلى الجواب المذكور، وذلك بناء على أن علة الكائنية ذات الصفة لا قيامها كما مر وأنت خبير بأنه تعسف ظاهر ومخالف لتوجيه تلميذه الذي هو أعلم بمراده
It may be said that dependence in the sense of the impossibility of separation does not necessitate impossible circularity. Its exposition according to what is in the book al-Arba‘īn is that if by 'dependence' is meant the necessity of the dependant being posterior to its dependee, then from the absence of dependence it does not follow that the cause can exist without the effect. And if by it is meant the impossibility of its existence without the dependee, then from dependence in this sense circularity does not follow, because it is possible that the cause and effect are mutually entailing while the effect is in need of its cause without the reverse. The author said: 'It' i.e., what this objector mentioned, is not directed at the words of the Imam; that will become clear to you if you reflect. One of his students explained it by saying: Mere mutual inseparability, even if it does not entail impossible circularity, yet here there is another matter that does entail it, for the Imam explicitly stated that the subsistence of an attribute in a thing means that its spatialization is dependent on its spatialization, and there is no doubt that the spatialization of the substance is dependent on the subsistence of the attribute because it is its cause, so impossible circularity follows. And this is refuted: firstly, because there is no explicit statement of that meaning in this copy; rather, in it, the subsistence of the aforementioned attribute in the substance is either dependent... the two possibilities are the invalidity of the implication on one possibility and the invalidity of the consequent on another possibility, decisively cutting off the source of the doubt. The other: His statement: 'And this is refuted, etc.' It can be said: The intended meaning of the interpreter is that the disjunction in the meaning of qiyām is such that it can be said about the subsistence of the attribute: if it depends on spatialization, i.e., interpreted as dependence in spatialization, then circularity follows—that is apparent. And if it does not depend on it, i.e., interpreted as attributive specification of the substance without occurrence in the locus, as in the case of the Necessary Exalted, then the subsistence is not a necessitating cause. Then the outcome of the two copies is the same, except that in the two copies the possibility of non-dependence was omitted because it is contrary to the well-known meaning of qiyām, and it was mentioned for support. Then the refutation is repelled by three points: First, because explicit statement is not necessary since it is well-known, and this objector's inquiry about the meaning of dependence is not valid because we have explained that what is meant by it is the dependence of posteriority, and the disjunction is based on the interpretation of qiyām. Second, because he did not make the adherence to the meaning of qiyām a report of another evidence he adduced in dependence based on the two interpretations of qiyām; it is in reality adherence to the meaning of qiyām as in the other copy. Third, because what he mentioned of the aspect is an explanation of the intention of the Imam so that what he mentioned does not apply to him. His statement: 'It is not directed' when said: 'If you reflect, it means that the objection of the Imam is not valid, so there is no need for the answer mentioned.' This is based on the fact that the cause of spatialization is the essence of the attribute, not its subsistence, as has passed. And you are aware that this is an apparent contortion and contradicts the interpretation of his student who is more knowledgeable of his intent.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is a discussion and refutation of a claim about the meaning of tawaqquf (dependence). It begins with the claim that if tawaqquf is understood as the impossibility of separation (i.e., inseparable concomitance), it does not entail impossible circularity. The exposition from al-Arba‘īn distinguishes two senses: (1) necessary posteriority, and (2) impossibility of existence without the dependee. In the second sense, circularity does not necessarily follow because cause and effect can be mutually entailing without the effect being dependent on the cause in the same way. The author (al-muṣannif) responds that this claim is not directed at Imam al-Razi's words. A student of al-Razi offered an interpretation: even if mutual inseparability does not entail circularity, there is another factor—the explicit statement of al-Razi that subsistence (qiyām) means derivative spatialization—which leads to circularity. The commentator then refutes this interpretation: first, this explicit statement is not present in the current copy; second, the student's interpretation misconstrues the disjunction in the meaning of qiyām. The passage concludes by noting that the correct view is that the cause is the attribute's essence, not its subsistence, and that the student's interpretation strains the text.

Key Terms
قيام
تحيز
اختصاص ناعت

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that if a scholar's student interprets his words in a certain way, that interpretation must be correct.
Correction: The commentator shows that the student's interpretation is not supported by the text of the available copy.

Checkpoint
What is the first refutation of the student's interpretation?
A) The student misquoted al-Razi
B) There is no explicit statement of that meaning in this copy
C) The student misunderstood circularity
D) The two copies of al-Arba‘īn are identical

Key Takeaway
The student's interpretation is refuted primarily because the explicit statement upon which it relies is not present in the existing text.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a claim about a religious text based solely on the authority of a teacher or scholar, without verifying it yourself? When will you start checking the original sources?
?
This lesson shows that even a student of a great imam can misrepresent his teacher's words. Are you cautious when you quote scholars? Do you ensure you have seen the exact wording before passing on an interpretation?
?
The refutation cuts doubt by pointing to the actual text. In your own beliefs, have you built conclusions on assumptions about what is 'implied' without evidence? How can you train yourself to demand explicit proof?

The Remaining Refutations and Conclusion
Further refutations and final resolution of the debate on tawaqquf and qiyām.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second and third refutations of the student's interpretation, and the final conclusion that the cause is the attribute's essence, not its subsistence.

Core Idea
The student's interpretation is refuted on multiple grounds, and the correct view traces causation to the attribute's essence.

The commentator continues his rebuttal of the student's interpretation by offering two more refutations. He also addresses an alternative reading that attempts to salvage the student's argument. Ultimately, the passage concludes that the cause lies in the essence of the attribute, not in its subsistence, and the student's forced interpretation fails.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic from 'الأخرى قوله: (وهذا مردود إلخ)' to the end, along with translation.
This passage contains the remaining refutations and the concluding remark.
2
Understand the 'other' explanation
An attempt to explain the student's intention: he might have been basing his argument on the well-known meaning of qiyām despite its absence in the text.
3
Follow the three points of refutation
The commentator gives three reasons why this explanation fails: explicit statement is not required because it is well-known, etc.
4
Grasp the final conclusion
The passage ends by stating that the correct view is that the cause is the essence of the attribute, and the student's interpretation is a contortion of the text.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a theological debate that seems purely abstract, like the one about qiyām, do you tend to dismiss it as irrelevant to your faith? Read the passage again. Are you willing to submit your intellect to the rigorous analysis required to distinguish truth from distortion, even when it feels tedious? What will you do this week to train your mind to respect the depth of such debates rather than avoid them?
?
The passage concludes that the cause of spatialization is the attribute's essence, not its subsistence. This means that what an attribute is (its essence) determines its causal power, not how it 'sits' in a substance. In your own life, do you focus more on the outward 'subsistence' of your deeds—how they look to others—or on their inner essence of sincerity and intention? Which one truly causes change in your soul? Make a concrete decision today to prioritize essence over appearance in one action.
?
The student's interpretation is described as a 'forced reading' that contradicts the more authoritative view of his teacher. Have you ever held onto an interpretation of a religious text because it was convenient or comfortable, even when deeper scholars pointed you elsewhere? What is one belief or practice you need to re-examine with humility, letting go of your own 'contortion' to submit to the way of the people of knowledge? Write down your answer and share it with a trusted teacher.

The Objection on Tawaqquf and Al-Iji's Response
Al-Iji presents an objection and responds that it does not apply.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that tawaqquf (dependency) does not necessarily entail an impossible cycle, and how al-Iji argues that the objection does not apply to his specific argument.

Core Idea
Not all dependency creates a vicious cycle; context defines the problem.

In the ongoing discussion about causality and attributes, an objector challenges the notion that tawaqquf (dependency) must lead to an impossible cycle (dawr). The objector distinguishes two meanings of tawaqquf and argues that neither necessarily creates a cycle. Al-Iji responds that the objection misses the specific dependency in his argument, as explained by his students. This lesson unpacks the objection and the initial response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the objector's argument and the author's response come through.
Notice the technical terms like انفكاك, توقف, and الدور الممتنع.
2
Identify the two meanings of tawaqquf
The objector explains two possible meanings of tawaqquf: (1) necessary posteriority of the dependent, and (2) impossibility of existence without the thing depended upon. Understand each.
These distinctions are crucial for the objection.
3
Examine why the objector says no cycle follows
The objector argues that under either meaning, tawaqquf does not necessitate an impossible cycle. For the first, absence of tawaqquf does not imply cause without effect; for the second, cause and effect could be mutually inseparable without a cycle.
This is the core of the objection.
4
Analyze al-Iji's response
Al-Iji says the objection does not apply. A student explains that in this specific case, the Imam's definition of qiyam creates a dependency that does lead to a cycle: the attribute's location depends on the substance's location, yet the substance's location depends on the attribute's subsistence.
The response shows why the objection is not relevant to the original argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
انفكاك العلة التي هي تلك الصفة القائمة بالجوهر عن المعلول الذي هو الحصول في الحيز، لأنه لما لم يتوقف قيامها به على الحصول أمكن القيام بدون الحصول، فأمكن أن توجد تلك الصفة قائمة بالجوهر خالية عن معلولها الذي هو الحصول. وقد يقال: إن التوقف بمعنى عدم جواز الانفكاك لا يوجب دوراً ممتنعاً، وتقريره على ما في كتاب الأربعين، أنه إن عني بالتوقف وجوب تأخر الموقوف عن الموقوف عليه لم يلزم من عدم التوقف إمكان حصول العلة بدون المعلول، وإن عني به عدم جواز وجوده بدون الموقوف عليه لم يلزم من التوقف بهذا المعنى الدور لجواز أن تكون العلة والمعلول متلازمين مع كون المعلول محتاجاً إلى علته بلا عكس. قال المصنف: (وهو) أي ما ذكره هذا القائل غير وارد على كلام الإمام يظهر ذلك عليك إذا تأملت. وقد وجهه بعض تلامذته بأن مجرد امتناع الانفكاك من الجانبين، وإن لم يستلزم دوراً ممتنعاً، إلا أن هاهنا أمراً آخر يستلزمه، إذ قد صرح الإمام بأن قيام الصفة بالشيء معناه أن تحيزها تابع لتحيزه، ولا شك أن تحيز الجوهر تابع لقيام الصفة لكونه علة له فيلزم الدور الممتنع.
The separation of the cause—which is that attribute subsisting in the substance—from the effect—which is obtaining in location—because since its subsistence in it does not depend on obtaining, subsistence without obtaining is possible, so it is possible for that attribute to exist subsisting in the substance devoid of its effect which is obtaining. And it is said: 'Tawaqquf' in the sense of the impossibility of separation does not necessitate an impossible cycle. Its explanation according to what is in the book 'Al-Arba'īn' is that if by 'tawaqquf' is meant the necessary delay of the dependent upon the thing depended upon, then the absence of tawaqquf does not entail the possibility of the cause existing without the effect; and if by it is meant the impossibility of its existence without the thing depended upon, then tawaqquf in this sense does not necessitate a cycle because it is possible for the cause and effect to be mutually inseparable while the effect is in need of its cause without the reverse. The author said: 'This—i.e., what this objector mentioned—does not apply to the Imam's statement; that becomes clear to you when you reflect.' And some of his students explained it by saying that mere impossibility of separation from both sides, even if it does not necessitate an impossible cycle, yet here there is another matter that necessitates it, since the Imam explicitly stated that the subsistence of an attribute in a thing means that its location is dependent on its location, and there is no doubt that the location of the substance is dependent on the subsistence of the attribute because it is its cause, so the impossible cycle follows.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-response within a larger argument about causality in attributes. The objection challenges the claim that a cause must be separable from its effect, arguing that if 'tawaqquf' (dependency) means mere inability to separate, then a cycle (dawr) does not necessarily follow. The objector explains two possible meanings of tawaqquf: (1) the necessary posteriority of the dependent, which would not guarantee that absence of tawaqquf allows the cause to exist without the effect; (2) the impossibility of existence without the thing depended upon, which also does not entail a cycle because cause and effect could be mutually inseparable. The author (al-Iji) responds that this objection does not apply to his argument, and a student further argues that in this specific case, the Imam's definition of qiyām (subsistence) entails a different kind of dependency that does create a cycle: the attribute's location follows the substance's location, yet the substance's location follows the attribute's subsistence, thus a cycle. The commentary then prepares for a rebuttal.

Key Terms
انفكاك
التوقف
الدور الممتنع
الموقوف
الموقوف عليه
تعليل
قيام الصفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any mutual dependency or inseparability between cause and effect constitutes a vicious cycle.
Correction: The objection clarifies that tawaqquf in the sense of impossibility of separation does not necessarily entail an impossible cycle; cause and effect can be mutually inseparable without a problematic cycle, depending on the definition of tawaqquf.

Checkpoint
According to the objection in this passage, what does the objector claim about 'tawaqquf'?
A) It must always entail a cycle
B) It does not necessarily entail an impossible cycle
C) It is the same as the author's concept
D) It is irrelevant to causality

Key Takeaway
The objection shows that tawaqquf does not automatically entail a cycle; al-Iji's response refines the argument by showing why a cycle arises from his specific definition of attribute subsistence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that two things that are inseparable must be in a vicious cycle? Can you identify a belief or relationship in your life where you have misapplied this assumption, and how can you now examine it more carefully?
?
The student showed that the objection failed because of a specific definition. Are there any areas of your faith where you have allowed vague definitions to hide a potential contradiction? What would it take for you to define your terms as precisely as these scholars did?
?
This lesson reveals how careful reasoning can protect deeper truths. When was the last time you engaged in such rigorous thinking about your beliefs? Will you commit to examining one key belief this week with the same level of scrutiny?

Commentator's Threefold Refutation
Commentator refutes student's tawjih in three points.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three specific reasons the commentator gives to reject the student's defense of the author's response.

Core Idea
Three sharp critiques dismantle a student's attempted defense.

The commentator provides a crisp rebuttal of the student's tawjīh (interpretation) that tried to explain why the author's argument holds. Each of the three points targets a different weakness: lack of textual support, mischaracterization of the argument's independence, and selection of an already-refuted horn. Understanding these critiques sharpens skills in reading kalam texts and evaluating arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the three points
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the clear enumeration: أولاً, ثانياً, ثالثاً.
Each point is a separate argument.
2
Examine the first point: no explicit text
The commentator says the text does not explicitly define qiyām as the student claimed; it only presents a dilemma. This undercuts the student's textual basis.
3
Analyze the second point: independence of the meaning
Adhering to a specific meaning of qiyām is an independent argument, not part of another proof. The student's approach conflates distinct lines of reasoning.
4
Understand the third point: wrong horn chosen
The student selects the first meaning of tawaqquf (delayed dependency), but the objector's refutation already targeted that horn, so the student fails to counter the objection.
The second horn remains the real challenge.
5
Reflect on the refutation's structure
Consider how the commentator systematically addresses textual, logical, and contextual weaknesses. This is a model of concise scholarly critique.

وهذا مردود أما أولاً، فلأنه لا تصريح بذلك المعنى في هذه النسخة بل فيها أن قيام الصفة المذكورة بالجوهر، إما أن يتوقف على حصوله في الحيز أو لا يتوقف، فاستفسار هذا القائل متعلق بما ذكر فيها واعتراضه وارد عليه. وأما ثانياً فلأن التمسك بمعنى القيام وجه مستقل، كما في النسخة الأخرى في وجه لجعله جزءاً لدليل آخر. وأما ثالثاً: فلأن هذا التوجيه اختيار للشق الأول، وهو قوله: إن عني بالتوقف وجوب تأخر الموقوف إلخ، وهو أن قيام الصفة متوقف على الحصول توقف تأخر، وقد أبطله هذا القائل بأن عدمه لا يستلزم إمكان وجود العلة بدون المعلول، كما نقلناه عنه لا للشق الثاني المذكور في الكتاب.
This is refuted: First, because there is no explicit mention of that meaning in this version; rather, it states that the subsistence of the aforementioned attribute in the substance either depends on its obtaining in location or does not depend, so the question of this objector is related to what is mentioned in it, and his objection is valid against it. Second, because adhering to the meaning of qiyām is an independent aspect, as in the other version, in terms of making it part of another proof. Third, because this explanation chooses the first horn, which is his statement: 'If by tawaqquf is meant the necessary delay of the dependent,' etc., which is that the subsistence of the attribute is dependent on obtaining in the sense of delayed dependency; and this objector invalidated it by saying that its absence does not entail the possibility of the cause existing without the effect, as we have transmitted from him—not the second horn mentioned in the book.
Source pages p. 172

Commentary
This is a refutation by the commentator of a student's attempt to defend the author's response. The commentator gives three reasons. First: The student claimed that Imam al-Iji explicitly defined qiyām as the attribute's location following the substance's location, but the text in this version does not contain that explicit definition; it only says that the attribute's subsistence either depends on obtaining in location or not. So the objector's question is actually relevant to what is in the text, and his objection stands. Second: Adhering to a particular meaning of qiyām is an independent argument; it is not part of another proof as the student implied. Third: The student's explanation only chooses the first horn of the dilemma (tawaqquf as delayed dependency), but the objector's refutation was actually directed at that horn, not the second horn, so the student's argument fails to address the objection properly.

Proof and Refutation of 'Kawn' as Cause
Al-Iji presents and refutes a proof that 'kawn' is the cause of 'ka'inah'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the dialectical proof for the attribute 'kawn' as the cause of a substance's specific location, and the Ash'ari refutation that God's direct creation suffices.

Core Idea
Not every necessity requires an attribute—God's direct creation is sufficient.

A central debate in kalam concerns whether spatial attributes like 'kawn' (being-in-a-location) are real, causally effective qualities. Proponents argue that since a substance's essence only necessitates existence in some location, a specific location must be caused by a distinct attribute. The Ash'ari response, however, denies the need for such an attribute, affirming that God directly creates the substance in its particular location. This lesson presents the full argument and answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the full Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the flow of the argument: first the proof from the proponents, then the response introduced by 'But'.
Notice the technical terms: kawn, ka'inah, ahyaz, mutahayyiz, jawhar.
2
Understand the proponents' proof
The proponents argue that a substance's essence only necessitates existence in some location, not a specific one. Therefore a specific location requires an external cause, which they identify as kawn—a relation that necessitates that specific location.
They distinguish between ka'inah (the result) and kawn (the cause).
3
Examine al-Iji's refutation
Al-Iji concedes the need for an external cause but denies that this cause is an attribute like kawn. Instead, the specific location is directly created by God (khalq). Thus the proof for kawn as a separate attribute fails.
The refutation does not deny the logical need for a cause, only that it must be an inherent attribute.
4
Reflect on the theological implications
Consider why the Ash'ari position prioritizes divine omnipotence and avoids multiplying attributes unnecessarily. This reflects a broader commitment to tawhid and simplicity in divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه: على ما يتمسك به من أثبت الكون علة للكائنية مع الجواب عنه. أما التمسك فهو أنهم قالوا: الأحياز الجزئية الممكنة للمتحيز الذي هو الجوهر نسبتها إليه سواء، فإن ذات الجوهر تقتضي حصوله في حيز ما أي حيز كان، وإنما يقتضي حصوله في حيز ما مخصوص بحسب ما يقارنه من شرط يعينه، أي يعين ذلك الحيز المخصوص وحصوله فيه. فهناك أمران أحدهما الكائنية، أعني الحصول في الحيز المخصوص، وثانيهما الكون الذي هو نسبته، أي المقتضي لنسبته إلى الحيز المخصوص وحصوله فيه، فالفرق بين الكون الذي هو مقتضى وبين الحصول في الحيز أعني الكائنية المقتضاة ظاهر. وأما الجواب فهو قوله: لكن أي نحن نسلم أن نسبة الجوهر إلى الأحياز الممكنة على السوية، وأنه لا بد لحصوله في حيز معين من مقتضى خارج عن ذاته، لكن الكلام في ثبوت ذلك المقتضى وأنه ماذا، فنحن لا نسلم أن حصوله في الحيز معلل بصفة أخرى قائمة به مسماة بالكون كما يزعمون، فإن الحصول في الحيز المخصوص إنما يثبت له عندنا بخلق الله تعالى، فلا حاجة إلى إثبات صفة أخرى له.
Note: Regarding what is adhered to by those who affirm that kawn is the cause of ka'inah, along with the answer to it. As for the adherence, they said: The particular possible locations for the located being (which is the substance) have equal relation to it, for the essence of the substance necessitates its obtaining in some location, any location, and it only necessitates its obtaining in a particular location according to a condition that accompanies it and specifies it, i.e., specifies that particular location and its obtaining in it. So there are two things: one is ka'inah, i.e., obtaining in the particular location, and the second is kawn, which is its relation, i.e., that which necessitates its relation to the particular location and its obtaining in it. So the difference between kawn which is the necessitator and obtaining in the location i.e., ka'inah which is necessitated, is clear. As for the answer, it is his statement: 'But'—i.e., we concede that the relation of the substance to possible locations is equal, and that it needs a necessitator external to its essence for its obtaining in a specific location, but the discussion is about the existence of that necessitator and what it is. Therefore we do not concede that its obtaining in a location is caused by another attribute subsisting in it named kawn as they claim, for obtaining in the particular location is affirmed for it, according to us, by the creation of God Almighty, so there is no need to affirm another attribute for it.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is a proof-and-refutation concerning the causal relationship between 'kawn' (spatial attribute) and 'ka'inah' (existentiality in a particular location). The proponents argue that the substance's essence only necessitates existence in some location, not a specific one, so for a specific location a cause is needed. They distinguish between kawn (the relation that specifies the location) and ka'inah (the actual obtaining in that location), claiming kawn is the cause. The response (given by al-Iji) concedes the need for an external cause but denies that this cause is an attribute called kawn. According to the Ash'ari view, the specific location is brought about directly by God's creation (khalq), not by an inherent attribute. Thus the proof for kawn as a separate attribute is rejected.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that since a substance needs a cause for its specific location, that cause must be an inherent attribute like kawn.
Correction: The Ash'ari response shows that God's direct creation suffices; no additional attribute is needed.

Checkpoint

B) The substance's own essence
C) The relation between substance and location
D) God's creation (khalq)

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari school denies the need for an attribute called kawn to cause a substance's specific location; God's direct creation is the sufficient cause.

The Argument for Kaun as Cause and Its Refutation
Al-Jurjani presents the proof for kaun as a cause and refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that kaun is the cause of an atom's specific location and how the Ashʿarī refute it by appealing to direct divine creation.

Core Idea
The atom's specific place is directly from God, not from a separate attribute called kaun.

In this note, al-Jurjani presents an argument by those who claim that an additional accident, kaun, is the cause of an atom's particular location. He then refutes this by asserting that the specific location is simply created by God, eliminating the need for a separate causal attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two sides
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify where the argument for kaun ends and where the refutation begins.
The argument is introduced by 'أما التمسك' and the refutation by 'وأما الجواب'.
2
Understand the logic of the argument
The argument claims that since the atom's essence requires some place but not a specific one, there must be a cause (kaun) that specifies the location. Notice how they distinguish between kā'iniyya (the effect) and kaun (the cause).
3
Examine the refutation
The refutation concedes the premises but denies the conclusion. It says the specific location is not caused by a separate attribute but is directly created by God. Consider why this matters for Ashʿarī theology.
This shows the Ashʿarī commitment to God's direct action in all particulars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(تنبيه) على ما يتمسك به من أثبت الكون علة للكائنية مع الجواب عنه أما التمسك فهو أنهم قالوا: ( الأحياز الجزئية الممكنة للمتحيز) الذي هو الجوهر (نسبتها إليه سواء فإنَّ ذات الجوهر تقتضي حصوله في حيز ما أي حيز كان ( وإنما يقتضي حصوله في حيز (ما مخصوص ) بحسب ما يقارنه من شرط يعينه ) أي يعين ذلك الحيز المخصوص وحصوله فيه فهناك أمران أحدهما الكائنية، أعني الحصول في الحيز المخصوص (و) ثانيهما ( الكون ( الذي ) هو نسبته ) أي المقتضي لنسبته إلى الحيز المخصوص) وحصوله فيه ( فالفرق ) بين الكون الذي هو مقتضى وبين الحصول في الحيز أعني الكائنية المقتضاة ( ظاهر) ، وأما الجواب فهو قوله : (لكن) أي نحن نسلم أن نسبة الجوهر إلى الأحياز الممكنة على السوية، وأنه لا بد لحصوله في حيز معين من مقتضى خارج عن ذاته لكن الكلام في ثبوت ذلك المقتضى) وأنه ما ذا فنحن لا نسلم أن حصوله في الحيز معلل بصفة أخرى قائمة به مسماة بالكون كما يزعمون ) فإن الحصول في الحيز ( المخصوص ) إنما يثبت له ( عندنا بخلق الله تعالى ( فلا حاجة إلى إثبات صفة أخرى له .
A note on what those who affirm kaun as a cause for kā'iniyya rely on, along with the answer to it. As for the reliance, it is that they said: 'The particular possible places for the spatially located (which is the atom) have equal relation to it; for the essence of the atom necessitates its occurrence in some place, any place; and it only necessitates its occurrence in a particular place according to what accompanies it of a condition that specifies it.' That is, it specifies that particular place and its occurrence in it. So there are two things: one is kā'iniyya, i.e., the occurrence in the particular place; and the second is kaun, which is its relation (i.e., the cause of its relation to the particular place and its occurrence in it). So the distinction between kaun, which is the cause, and the occurrence in the place, i.e., the caused kā'iniyya, is apparent. As for the answer, it is his statement: 'But' – i.e., we concede that the relation of the atom to the possible places is equal, and that it is necessary for its occurrence in a specific place to have a cause external to its essence; but the discussion is about the existence of that cause and what it is. So we do not concede that its occurrence in the place is caused by another attribute subsisting in it called kaun, as they claim. For the occurrence in the particular place is only established for it, in our view, by God's creation. So there is no need to establish another attribute for it.
Source pages p. 173

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer structure. It begins with a 'tanbih' (note) regarding the argument used by those who affirm that kaun (the accident of being in a place) is the cause of kā'iniyya (the state of being in a specific place). The proof is presented: the parts of space are equally related to the atom, so the atom's essence requires it to be in some place, but a specific place requires a condition. Hence there are two things: kā'iniyya (the specific location) and kaun (the relation to that location). The distinction between them is clear. The answer (the refutation) concedes the premise about equal relation but denies that the specific location is caused by a distinct quality called kaun; rather, it is by direct creation of God, so no need for an extra attribute.

Correction: The Ashʿarī refutation shows that the specific location can be directly created by God, so no separate attribute kaun is needed.

Motion as Sum of Two Kawns: A Refuted View
Presents and refutes a specific definition of motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the view that motion is the sum of two kawns in two places was refuted by mutakallimun.

Core Idea
A single kawn cannot be part of both motion and stillness.

Some early mutakallimun defined motion as the sum of two kawns in two instants in two places, and stillness similarly in one place. This lesson presents that view and the immediate refutation that it would force a single kawn to be part of both categories, losing essential distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the view and refutation
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Identify the definition of motion and stillness, and note the refutation's key point.
Remember: kawn is a technical term for 'occurrence' or 'state'.
2
Analyze the refutation
The refutation argues that the first kawn in the second place would be part of both motion and stillness, destroying their essential difference. Consider why this is problematic.
3
Connect to broader kalām
This refutation ties into debates about the persistence of accidents (baqā' al-a'rāḍ). Think about how denying persistence affects the existence of motion and stillness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن بعض المتكلمين قالوا: الحركة مجموع كونين في آنين في مكانين، والسكون مجموع كونين في آنين في مكان واحد، ورد عليه بأنه يلزم أن يكون الكون الأول في المكان الثاني جزءاً من الحركة والسكون معاً، ولا يتمايزان بالذات على أنهم اتفقوا على وجود الكون بأنواعه الأربعة ولا وجود للحركة والسكون على هذا القول عند من لا يقول ببقاء الأعراض. قوله: (فهؤلاء لم يعتبروا إلخ) فالقسمة عندهم أن الكون في الحيز إن كان مسبوقاً بالكون في حيز آخر بلا واسطة فحركة أو لا فسكون سواء لا يكون مسبوقاً بكون أصلاً أو مسبوقاً بكون في ذلك الحيز، ففي السكون لا يعتبر المسبوقية، فالكون الواحد يجوز أن يكون سكوناً وحركة باعتبارين كما سيصرح بذلك، وهو معنى قوله: فيلزمهم تركيب الحركة أي الممتدة من السكنات لا الحركة بمعنى الكون المخصوص، فإنه لا جزء له وهذا الإطلاق عند المتكلمين كالإطلاق على التوسط والقطع عند الحكماء. قوله: (فإن أحد الضدين إلخ) لأن الضدين لا بد أن يتعاقبا على محل واحد فإن كان أحدهما جزءاً محمولاً على الآخر لزم اجتماعهما فيما يصدق عليه أحدهما، والمحمول على الشيء محمول عليه لذلك الشيء إذا كان الحمل متعارفاً، وإن كان جزءاً غير محمول لم يتعاقبا مطلقاً، فاختار الأكثرون ما ذكره المصنف من أن الحركة حصول أول في حيز ثان، والسكون حصول ثان في حيز أول، واعترض عليه على القولين ببقاء الأكوان بأنه يلزم أن يكون كون واحد بعينه حركة وسكوناً، ويكون الاختلاف بينهما كالاختلاف بين الشيخ والشباب، وقد يلتزم ذلك بناء على إطباقهم على أن اختلاف أنواع الكون بالعوارض الاعتبارية لا الفصول المقومة. قوله: (فهؤلاء لم يعتبروا في السكون اللبث إلخ) وعلى هذا لا يتم ما ذكره في طريق الحصر بل طريقه أن يقال: إنه إن كان مسبوقاً بحصوله في حيز آخر فحركة وإلا فسكون. قوله: (فيلزمهم تركب الحركة من السكنات فإن قلت لا يلزم من عدم اعتبار اللبث والمسبوقية بكون آخر في ذلك الحيز في الكون، عدم اعتبار اللامسبوقية بكون آخر في حيز آخر فتفريع لزوم تركب الحركة الممتدة من السكنات على عدم اعتبارهما فيه ليس كما ينبغي ما ذكره الشارح مبني على قولهم بتماثل الحصول الأول والثاني في الحيز الأول، فكذا في الحيز الثاني فيصح التفريع المذكور كما لا يخفى. قوله: (فإن أحد الضدين لا يكون جزءاً للآخر) قيل: هذا كلام مشهور منهم، وليس لهم دليل آخر عليه كيف والبلقة عند السواد والبياض مع أنهما يقومان، وقد سبق ما فيه فتذكر.
Know that some mutakallimun said: motion is the sum of two kawns in two instants in two places, and stillness is the sum of two kawns in two instants in one place. It was refuted by saying that then the first kawn in the second place would be part of both motion and stillness, and they would not be distinguished essentially, despite their agreement on the existence of the four types of kawn, and that motion and stillness would not exist according to this view for those who do not hold the persistence of accidents. His statement: (these did not consider, etc.) So the division according to them is that the kawn in the place, if preceded by a kawn in another place without mediation, is motion; otherwise it is stillness, whether it is not preceded by any kawn at all or preceded by a kawn in that same place. So in stillness, being preceded is not considered; therefore a single kawn can be both stillness and motion under two aspects, as he will clearly state, and that is the meaning of his statement: so it follows that motion is composed of stillness, i.e., extended motion composed of stillnesses, not motion in the sense of a specific kawn, for it has no part. And this usage among the mutakallimun is like the usage of 'tawassut' and 'qat' among the philosophers. His statement: (for one of two opposites cannot be part of the other) because two opposites must alternate upon a single subject; if one were a part predicated of the other, they would coexist in that of which that one is true, and what is predicated of a thing is predicated of that thing if the predication is conventional; if it is a non-predicated part, they do not alternate at all. So the majority chose what the author mentioned: that motion is a first occurrence in a second place, and stillness is a second occurrence in a first place. And an objection was raised against both views holding the persistence of kawns, that it would follow that one and the same kawn would be both motion and stillness, the difference between them being like the difference between youth and old age, and this may be accepted based on their consensus that the difference between types of kawn is by accidental considerations (al-awarid al-i'tibariyya) not by constitutive essential differences. His statement: (these did not consider in stillness remaining (lubth), etc.) And according to this, what he mentioned in the method of division is not complete; rather the method is to say: if it is preceded by its occurrence in another place it is motion, otherwise stillness. His statement: (so it follows that motion is composed of stillness. If you say: not considering the remaining and being preceded by another kawn in that place in the kawn does not entail not considering the non-precedence by another kawn in another place, so the inference that extended motion is composed of stillness from their not considering them is not correct;) what the commentator mentioned is based on their view that the first and second occurrences in the first place are similar, and likewise in the second place, so the said inference is valid, as is not hidden. His statement: (for one of two opposites cannot be part of the other) it is said: this is a well-known statement of theirs, and they have no other proof for it; how about 'balqa' (?) concerning black and white, even though they are accidents? And what has preceded about it is remembered.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage debates the nature of motion and stillness, specifically whether extended motion can be composed of successive stillnesses (sakanat). The commentator begins by reporting a view of some mutakallimun that motion is the sum of two kawns in two instants in two places, and stillness is the sum of two kawns in two instants in one place. This is refuted because it would make the first kawn in the second place part of both motion and stillness, losing essential distinction. Moreover, those who deny the persistence of accidents would not allow motion and stillness to exist at all under this view. The commentary then explains the reasoning of those who do not consider 'remaining' (lubth) in stillness: for them, a kawn is motion if preceded by a kawn in another place without mediation; otherwise it is stillness, regardless of whether it is preceded by any kawn or by a kawn in the same place. Consequently, a single kawn could be both stillness and motion under two aspects, leading to the conclusion that extended motion is composed of stillnesses (sakanat). The commentator clarifies that this does not apply to motion as a specific kawn (which has no parts) but to extended motion, analogous to how philosophers distinguish 'tawassut' and 'qat'. The objector then argues that motion cannot be composed of stillness because they are opposites, and opposites cannot be parts of each other. The response is that motion and stillness are not absolute opposites; they can alternate. The majority ultimately chose the author's definition: motion as a first occurrence in a second place and stillness as a second occurrence in a first place. An objection against both views is raised: if kawns persist, the same kawn could be both motion and stillness, differing only accidentally (like youth and old age), which some accept. The commentary further refines the division method, emphasizing that being preceded by an occurrence in another place is the criterion for motion, otherwise stillness. It defends the inference that motion is composed of stillness based on the similarity of first and second occurrences in the same place. Finally, it notes that the claim that one opposite cannot be part of another is a well-known assertion without other proof, citing the example of 'balqa' (?) with black and white.

Key Terms
حركة
سكون
كون
بقاء الأعراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Motion and stillness can be defined as sums of two kawns without issue.
Correction: Such definitions lead to the first kawn being part of both, undermining essential distinction, and face problems for those rejecting accident persistence.

Checkpoint
What is the main refutation of the view that motion is the sum of two kawns?
A) Motion cannot have parts.
B) The first kawn in the second place would be part of both motion and stillness.
C) Stillness does not exist.
D) Kawns are not real.

Key Takeaway
The definition of motion as a sum of two kawns fails because it makes the first kawn common to both motion and stillness.

Alternative Division and Composition of Motion from Stillness
Explains a division based on precedence and its consequence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how some mutakallimun defined motion and stillness by precedence, leading to the conclusion that extended motion is composed of stillnesses.

Core Idea
A single kawn can be both motion and stillness under two aspects.

After refuting the initial definition, the commentator presents an alternative division used by some: a kawn is motion if preceded by a kawn in another place, otherwise stillness. This leads to the idea that extended motion is composed of successive stillnesses, and raises the question of whether opposites can be parts of each other.

Guided Path
1
Understand the division criterion
Read how they classify a kawn as motion if preceded by a kawn in another place without mediation; otherwise it is stillness, regardless of whether it is preceded by any kawn or by a kawn in the same place.
This criterion does not consider 'remaining' (lubth) in stillness.
2
Grasp the implication: motion composed of stillness
Since a single kawn can be motion under one aspect and stillness under another, extended motion over multiple instants is seen as composed of stillnesses (sakanat). Note the distinction between extended motion and motion as a specific kawn.
This is analogous to philosophers' terms tawassut and qat.
3
Examine the objection about opposites
The passage then raises an objection: one opposite cannot be part of another. Consider how this challenges the composition view.

واعلم أن بعض المتكلمين قالوا: الحركة مجموع كونين في آنين في مكانين، والسكون مجموع كونين في آنين في مكان واحد، ورد عليه بأنه يلزم أن يكون الكون الأول في المكان الثاني جزءاً من الحركة والسكون معاً، ولا يتمايزان بالذات على أنهم اتفقوا على وجود الكون بأنواعه الأربعة ولا وجود للحركة والسكون على هذا القول عند من لا يقول ببقاء الأعراض. قوله: (فهؤلاء لم يعتبروا إلخ) فالقسمة عندهم أن الكون في الحيز إن كان مسبوقاً بالكون في حيز آخر بلا واسطة فحركة أو لا فسكون سواء لا يكون مسبوقاً بكون أصلاً أو مسبوقاً بكون في ذلك الحيز، ففي السكون لا يعتبر المسبوقية، فالكون الواحد يجوز أن يكون سكوناً وحركة باعتبارين كما سيصرح بذلك، وهو معنى قوله: فيلزمهم تركيب الحركة أي الممتدة من السكنات لا الحركة بمعنى الكون المخصوص، فإنه لا جزء له وهذا الإطلاق عند المتكلمين كالإطلاق على التوسط والقطع عند الحكماء. قوله: (فإن أحد الضدين إلخ) لأن الضدين لا بد أن يتعاقبا على محل واحد فإن كان أحدهما جزءاً محمولاً على الآخر لزم اجتماعهما فيما يصدق عليه أحدهما، والمحمول على الشيء محمول عليه لذلك الشيء إذا كان الحمل متعارفاً، وإن كان جزءاً غير محمول لم يتعاقبا مطلقاً، فاختار الأكثرون ما ذكره المصنف من أن الحركة حصول أول في حيز ثان، والسكون حصول ثان في حيز أول، واعترض عليه على القولين ببقاء الأكوان بأنه يلزم أن يكون كون واحد بعينه حركة وسكوناً، ويكون الاختلاف بينهما كالاختلاف بين الشيخ والشباب، وقد يلتزم ذلك بناء على إطباقهم على أن اختلاف أنواع الكون بالعوارض الاعتبارية لا الفصول المقومة. قوله: (فهؤلاء لم يعتبروا في السكون اللبث إلخ) وعلى هذا لا يتم ما ذكره في طريق الحصر بل طريقه أن يقال: إنه إن كان مسبوقاً بحصوله في حيز آخر فحركة وإلا فسكون. قوله: (فيلزمهم تركب الحركة من السكنات فإن قلت لا يلزم من عدم اعتبار اللبث والمسبوقية بكون آخر في ذلك الحيز في الكون، عدم اعتبار اللامسبوقية بكون آخر في حيز آخر فتفريع لزوم تركب الحركة الممتدة من السكنات على عدم اعتبارهما فيه ليس كما ينبغي ما ذكره الشارح مبني على قولهم بتماثل الحصول الأول والثاني في الحيز الأول، فكذا في الحيز الثاني فيصح التفريع المذكور كما لا يخفى. قوله: (فإن أحد الضدين لا يكون جزءاً للآخر) قيل: هذا كلام مشهور منهم، وليس لهم دليل آخر عليه كيف والبلقة عند السواد والبياض مع أنهما يقومان، وقد سبق ما فيه فتذكر.
Know that some mutakallimun said: motion is the sum of two kawns in two instants in two places, and stillness is the sum of two kawns in two instants in one place. It was refuted by saying that then the first kawn in the second place would be part of both motion and stillness, and they would not be distinguished essentially, despite their agreement on the existence of the four types of kawn, and that motion and stillness would not exist according to this view for those who do not hold the persistence of accidents. His statement: (these did not consider, etc.) So the division according to them is that the kawn in the place, if preceded by a kawn in another place without mediation, is motion; otherwise it is stillness, whether it is not preceded by any kawn at all or preceded by a kawn in that same place. So in stillness, being preceded is not considered; therefore a single kawn can be both stillness and motion under two aspects, as he will clearly state, and that is the meaning of his statement: so it follows that motion is composed of stillness, i.e., extended motion composed of stillnesses, not motion in the sense of a specific kawn, for it has no part. And this usage among the mutakallimun is like the usage of 'tawassut' and 'qat' among the philosophers. His statement: (for one of two opposites cannot be part of the other) because two opposites must alternate upon a single subject; if one were a part predicated of the other, they would coexist in that of which that one is true, and what is predicated of a thing is predicated of that thing if the predication is conventional; if it is a non-predicated part, they do not alternate at all. So the majority chose what the author mentioned: that motion is a first occurrence in a second place, and stillness is a second occurrence in a first place. And an objection was raised against both views holding the persistence of kawns, that it would follow that one and the same kawn would be both motion and stillness, the difference between them being like the difference between youth and old age, and this may be accepted based on their consensus that the difference between types of kawn is by accidental considerations (al-awarid al-i'tibariyya) not by constitutive essential differences. His statement: (these did not consider in stillness remaining (lubth), etc.) And according to this, what he mentioned in the method of division is not complete; rather the method is to say: if it is preceded by its occurrence in another place it is motion, otherwise stillness. His statement: (so it follows that motion is composed of stillness. If you say: not considering the remaining and being preceded by another kawn in that place in the kawn does not entail not considering the non-precedence by another kawn in another place, so the inference that extended motion is composed of stillness from their not considering them is not correct;) what the commentator mentioned is based on their view that the first and second occurrences in the first place are similar, and likewise in the second place, so the said inference is valid, as is not hidden. His statement: (for one of two opposites cannot be part of the other) it is said: this is a well-known statement of theirs, and they have no other proof for it; how about 'balqa' (?) concerning black and white, even though they are accidents? And what has preceded about it is remembered.

Commentary
This passage debates the nature of motion and stillness, specifically whether extended motion can be composed of successive stillnesses (sakanat). The commentator begins by reporting a view of some mutakallimun that motion is the sum of two kawns in two instants in two places, and stillness is the sum of two kawns in two instants in one place. This is refuted because it would make the first kawn in the second place part of both motion and stillness, losing essential distinction. Moreover, those who deny the persistence of accidents would not allow motion and stillness to exist at all under this view. The commentary then explains the reasoning of those who do not consider 'remaining' (lubth) in stillness: for them, a kawn is motion if preceded by a kawn in another place without mediation; otherwise it is stillness, regardless of whether it is preceded by any kawn or by a kawn in the same place. Consequently, a single kawn could be both stillness and motion under two aspects, leading to the conclusion that extended motion is composed of stillnesses (sakanat). The commentator clarifies that this does not apply to motion as a specific kawn (which has no parts) but to extended motion, analogous to how philosophers distinguish 'tawassut' and 'qat'. The objector then argues that motion cannot be composed of stillness because they are opposites, and opposites cannot be parts of each other. The response is that motion and stillness are not absolute opposites; they can alternate. The majority ultimately chose the author's definition: motion as a first occurrence in a second place and stillness as a second occurrence in a first place. An objection against both views is raised: if kawns persist, the same kawn could be both motion and stillness, differing only accidentally (like youth and old age), which some accept. The commentary further refines the division method, emphasizing that being preceded by an occurrence in another place is the criterion for motion, otherwise stillness. It defends the inference that motion is composed of stillness based on the similarity of first and second occurrences in the same place. Finally, it notes that the claim that one opposite cannot be part of another is a well-known assertion without other proof, citing the example of 'balqa' (?) with black and white.

?

Author's Definition and Objections on Persistence
Presents final definition, objections, and refinements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's definition of motion and stillness, and the objections raised against it concerning the persistence of kawns.

Core Idea
The author defines motion as first occurrence in a second place; stillness as second occurrence in a first place.

After discussing alternative views, the commentator presents the author's (al-Ījī) definition: motion is a first occurrence in a second place, stillness a second occurrence in a first place. This definition is chosen by the majority, but an objection is raised: if kawns persist, the same kawn could be both motion and stillness, differing only accidentally. The lesson also covers further refinements of the division and a note on the opposite-part argument.

Guided Path
1
Learn the author's definition
Read the definition: motion = first occurrence in second place; stillness = second occurrence in first place. Understand the temporal and locative aspects.
2
Understand the objection on persistence
If kawns persist (baqā' al-a'rāḍ), one and the same kawn could be both motion and stillness, like youth and old age. This objection is answered by some: differences are accidental, not essential.
The majority accept the definition despite this objection.
3
Review the refinement of division
The commentator refines the division: motion if preceded by occurrence in another place, otherwise stillness. He defends the inference of composition based on similarity of first and second occurrences.
4
Examine the note on opposite-part claim
The final remark notes that the claim 'one opposite cannot be part of another' is a famous assertion without other proof, citing the example of balqa with black and white.
The term 'balqa' is uncertain but likely relates to a mixed color.

?

?
The commentator defends an inference that may seem counterintuitive—that motion is composed of stillness. In your own life, what periods of stillness (or waiting) have you dismissed as wasted, when in fact they were building blocks for genuine progress?
?
The mutakallimun accepted an objection because they agreed on a principle about accidentals. Is there a deeply held belief you have been too rigid about, that might actually be a matter of perspective rather than essence? What would it cost you to admit that today?
