Ibn Sina's First Refutation: The Logical Connection
Ibn Sina presents his first logical proof against the impossibility of form change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Sina uses logical conversion to prove that some bodies subject to rectilinear motion must also undergo generation and corruption, thereby refuting the denial of form change.

Core Idea
Generation implies motion; motion proves generation.

Ibn Sina begins his refutation with a crisp logical argument. He first establishes a universal truth: anything that undergoes generation and corruption (change of form) also undergoes rectilinear motion (change of place). Then, by converting this proposition, he concludes that some bodies in rectilinear motion must undergo generation and corruption. This single step demolishes the claim that form change is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the first argument
Read the Arabic passage for the first aspect carefully, then the translation. Notice the logical structure: a universal affirmative proposition, then its conversion.
The phrase 'تنعكس هذه الموجبة الكلية بالعكس المستوى' is key to the logic.
2
Understand the universal premise
Ibn Sina asserts that every body that can undergo generation/corruption can also undergo rectilinear motion. This is the basis of the argument.
He says this is 'مبرهن فيما بعد' — proven later, but here he takes it as given.
3
Follow the conversion
The universal affirmative 'Every A is B' converts by simple conversion to 'Some B is A'. Here A = subject to generation/corruption, B = subject to rectilinear motion.
Simple conversion of a universal affirmative yields a particular affirmative.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The conclusion is that some bodies with rectilinear motion have generation/corruption. This proves that form change occurs in some bodies, contradicting the mutakallimun.
The argument does not require that all bodies with motion undergo change, only some.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأبطله أي قول ذلك البعض ابن سينا بوجهين : الأول : أنه مبرهن فيما بعد كما ستطلع عليه أن كل ما يصح عليه من الأجسام الكون والفساد أعني تبدل الصورة بصورة أخرى تصح عليه الحركة المستقيمة المقتضية لخروج الجسم عن مكانه وتنعكس هذه الموجبة الكلية بالعكس المستوى إلى قولنا : بعض ما يصح عليه الحركة المستقيمة عليه الكون والفساد فثبت صحة تبدل الصور في بعض الأجسام وبطل القول بكونه محالاً. الوجه الثاني : اختصاص الجزء المعين من الجسم العنصري كالماء مثلاً بحيز طبعاً أي بحيز معين من أجزاء الحيز الطبيعي لذلك الجسم، إنما يكون لصورته أي صورة ذلك الجزء وهذا أعني استناد ذلك الاختصاص إلى صورة ذلك الجزء أيضاً إنما يتصور إذا كانت تلك الصورة حادثة فإن ذلك الاختصاص لا يجوز أن يستند إلى ذات الصورة من حيث هي لأنا نشاهد أن الأجزاء المتساوية في الصورة حاصلة في أحياز متباينة ولا يجوز أن تستند إلى ناقل نقل ذلك الجزء إلى ذلك الحيز، إذ لو قدرنا عدم الناقل لكانت أجزاء العنصر حاصلة في أحيازها ولا بد لحصولها فيها حينئذ من سبب، ولا سبب سوى أن الجزء المعين كان في ابتداء تكونه حاصلاً في حيز تخصص به حدوثه عن الفاعل، واستمر بعد ذلك فيه باقتضاء صورته وإنما كان في ابتداء التكون حاصلاً في ذلك الحيز لكونه متصوراً بصورة أخرى حالها على قياس هذه الصورة وهكذا إلى ما لا نهاية له
And Ibn Sina refuted it – meaning the statement of that some – with two aspects: The first: It is proven later, as you will see, that every body that is subject to generation and corruption – meaning change of form to another form – is subject to rectilinear motion which necessitates the body leaving its place. And this universal affirmative proposition is converted by simple conversion to our statement: Some bodies subject to rectilinear motion are subject to generation and corruption. So the correctness of change of forms in some bodies is established, and the statement that it is impossible is false. The second aspect: The specific part of an elemental body, like water for example, being assigned to a natural place – meaning a specific part of the natural place for that body – is only due to its form, i.e. the form of that part. And this – meaning the attribution of that assignment to the form of that part – is also only conceivable if that form is caused. For that assignment cannot be attributed to the essence of the form as such, because we observe that parts equal in form occupy different places. And it cannot be attributed to a mover that moved that part to that place, because if we suppose the absence of a mover, the parts of the element would still be in their places, and they would necessarily have a cause for being there. And the only cause is that the specific part, at the beginning of its generation, was in a place specified by its origination from the agent, and it continues thereafter by requirement of its form. And it was at the beginning of generation in that place because it was formed with another form whose state is analogous to this form, and so on ad infinitum.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage presents Ibn Sina's two-pronged refutation of the mutakallimun who deny form change. The first argument uses a logical connection: it is proven that anything undergoing generation and corruption (change of form) also undergoes rectilinear motion (leaving its place). By converting the universal proposition, we get that some bodies undergoing rectilinear motion also undergo generation and corruption. This establishes that form change occurs in some bodies. The second argument concerns why a specific part of an element occupies a specific natural place. This assignment cannot be due to the form's essence alone, since identical forms would then occupy the same place. Nor can it be due to an external mover, as that would lead to an infinite regress. The only explanation is that the form is caused (حادثة) and originated at that place, with the part continuing there by its form's requirement. This implies that forms are created and changeable, contrary to the mutakallimun's claim.

كون وفساد
حركة مستقيمة
صورة
موجبة كلية
عكس مستو

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Forms are eternal and never change in substances.
Correction: Ibn Sina's first argument shows that some bodies in rectilinear motion undergo form change, proving that form change does occur.

B) Conversion of a universal affirmative (simple conversion)
C) Obversion
D) Contraposition

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's first refutation proves that form change must occur in some bodies by logically converting a universal truth about generation and motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

What universal truth about human nature might you be ignoring that could allow for that change in you? Will you test that truth today?
?
Ibn Sina's argument depends on a premise that he says will be proven later. Are there truths you accept on authority or intuition without examining their logical consequences? What would it cost you to follow those consequences wherever they lead?
?
The mutakallimun insisted on the impossibility of form change. When have you insisted that something in your life cannot change, only to find later that the real obstacle was your refusal to reason from what you already know? What step can you take this week to apply clear reasoning to a personal impasse?

Causality and Created Things

Ibn Sina's Second Refutation: The Necessity of Created Forms
Ibn Sina presents his second argument: place assignment implies forms are caused.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ibn Sina argues that the assignment of elemental parts to their natural places proves that forms are caused (accidental) and not eternal.

Core Idea
Why is this water here? Because its form is created.

Ibn Sina's second argument is more metaphysical. He asks: why does a specific part of an element, like a drop of water, occupy a particular natural place? The answer cannot be due to the form's essence (since identical forms occupy different places) nor due to an external mover (since that leads to infinite regress). The only remaining explanation is that the form itself is caused and originated at that place, implying that forms are created and subject to change.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument
Read the Arabic for the second aspect carefully, then the translation. Notice the causal reasoning: observation followed by elimination of alternatives.
Pay attention to the phrase 'إنما يتصور إذا كانت تلك الصورة حادثة'.
2
Identify the problem
The problem is: why is a specific part of an element (e.g., this water) in a specific natural place? The place assignment is linked to the form, but how?
Ibn Sina assumes elemental bodies have natural places (e.g., water tends downward).
3
Eliminate alternative explanations
He eliminates two possibilities: (a) the assignment is due to the form's essence, because identical forms would then occupy the same place, which is false; (b) it is due to an external mover, because if we suppose no mover, the parts would still be in place, requiring another cause.
The observation that equal forms are in different places refutes option (a).
4
Conclude that the form is caused
The only remaining cause is that the form itself is caused (حادثة) and the part originated at that place at the moment of generation, continuing there by the form's requirement. This leads to an infinite regress of forms, implying that forms are not eternal but created.
The regress is not vicious; it shows that each form depends on a prior form, ultimately tracing back to a first cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأبطله أي قول ذلك البعض ابن سينا بوجهين : الأول : أنه مبرهن فيما بعد كما ستطلع عليه أن كل ما يصح عليه من الأجسام الكون والفساد أعني تبدل الصورة بصورة أخرى تصح عليه الحركة المستقيمة المقتضية لخروج الجسم عن مكانه وتنعكس هذه الموجبة الكلية بالعكس المستوى إلى قولنا : بعض ما يصح عليه الحركة المستقيمة عليه الكون والفساد فثبت صحة تبدل الصور في بعض الأجسام وبطل القول بكونه محالاً. الوجه الثاني : اختصاص الجزء المعين من الجسم العنصري كالماء مثلاً بحيز طبعاً أي بحيز معين من أجزاء الحيز الطبيعي لذلك الجسم، إنما يكون لصورته أي صورة ذلك الجزء وهذا أعني استناد ذلك الاختصاص إلى صورة ذلك الجزء أيضاً إنما يتصور إذا كانت تلك الصورة حادثة فإن ذلك الاختصاص لا يجوز أن يستند إلى ذات الصورة من حيث هي لأنا نشاهد أن الأجزاء المتساوية في الصورة حاصلة في أحياز متباينة ولا يجوز أن تستند إلى ناقل نقل ذلك الجزء إلى ذلك الحيز، إذ لو قدرنا عدم الناقل لكانت أجزاء العنصر حاصلة في أحيازها ولا بد لحصولها فيها حينئذ من سبب، ولا سبب سوى أن الجزء المعين كان في ابتداء تكونه حاصلاً في حيز تخصص به حدوثه عن الفاعل، واستمر بعد ذلك فيه باقتضاء صورته وإنما كان في ابتداء التكون حاصلاً في ذلك الحيز لكونه متصوراً بصورة أخرى حالها على قياس هذه الصورة وهكذا إلى ما لا نهاية له

Proof that Substantial Change is Instantaneous
Al-Jurjani proves that generation and corruption cannot be gradual motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Jurjani argues that substantial change (kawn wa-fasād) cannot be gradual motion but must occur instantaneously.

Core Idea
Substantial forms do not admit degrees; their change is all at once, not piece by piece.

In this passage, al-Jurjani tackles a key issue in natural philosophy: how do substances change from one essence to another? He argues that such change cannot be a gradual process like heating or cooling, because forms themselves do not come in degrees. This has profound implications for understanding generation and corruption.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the logical structure. Then read the translation to ensure comprehension.
The passage ends abruptly due to page cut; the main argument is complete.
2
Identify the main claim
The author states that forms do not admit intensity or diminution, so gradual transition is impossible. Identify this key premise.
3
Understand the intermediate state argument
The argument uses the concept of an intermediate state during gradual change. If the species remains, no substantial change occurs; if it doesn't, the change is corruption, not motion.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Realize that the only alternative is that generation and corruption are instantaneous events, not gradual motions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المشاهدة) لدلالتهما على أن العناصر ينقلب بعضها إلى بعض (كما سيأتي) في الموقف الرابع ( ثم نقول : (في بيان أن تبديل الصورة بأخرى لا يجوز أن يكون تدريجياً فلا يكون حركة بل كوناً وفساداً ( الصور لا تقبل الاشتداد) بأن يتحرك محل الصورة إلى صورة أقوى منها ( ولا التنقص ) بأن يتحرك محلها إلى صورة أضعف منها على قياس الكيفيات التي تقع فيها الحركة، بل الصور لا تقبل الانتقال التدريجي مطلقاً بأن ينقل محل الصورة إلى صورة أخرى يسيراً يسيراً، سواء كانت الأخرى أقوى أو أضعف أو مساوية ( لأن في الوسط) أي في وسط الاشتداد أو التنقص، بل في وسط الانتقال التدريجي ( إن بقي نوعه ) أي نوع الجوهر المنتقل منه لم يكن التغير في الصورة أي لم يكن فيها اشتداد ولا تنقص بحسب ذاتها بل في لوازمها وصفاتها ولو قيل : إن بقي شخصه لكان أشمل وإن لم يبق نوعه أو
Observation indicates that the elements are transformed into one another, as will come in the fourth observatory. Then we say: In explanation that the replacement of one form (ṣūra) with another cannot be gradual, therefore it is not motion but generation and corruption (kawn wa-fasād). Forms do not admit intensity (ishtidād) — meaning that the locus of the form moves to a stronger form — nor diminution (tanaqquṣ) — meaning that its locus moves to a weaker form — analogous to qualities in which motion occurs. Rather, forms absolutely do not admit gradual transition, meaning that the locus of the form is transferred to another form little by little, whether the other form is stronger, weaker, or equal. For in the intermediate state (al-wasaṭ) — i.e., in the midst of intensity or diminution, rather in the midst of gradual transition — if its species (nawʿ) remains, i.e., the species of the substance from which it is transferred, then the change is not in the form, i.e., there is no intensity or diminution in its essence but rather in its necessary concomitants and attributes. And if it were said: if its individual (shakhṣ) remains, it would be more inclusive; and if its species does not remain, or [if its individual does not remain]...
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage is a proof that substantial change (generation and corruption) cannot occur as a gradual motion, but must be instantaneous. The author begins by referencing empirical observation that elements transform into each other. He then argues that forms (substantial forms) do not admit of intensity or diminution in the way that qualities do; you cannot gradually move from one form to another. If such gradual transition were possible, then during the intermediate state, either the original species (nawʿ) of the substance remains or it does not. If the species remains, then the change is not in the form itself but only in its accidental qualities, meaning no true substantial change has occurred. If the species does not remain, then the intermediate state would be a destruction of the form, which is not motion but corruption. Thus, true change of form must be instantaneous (generation and corruption), not gradual motion. The author also notes that the argument could be made more inclusive by considering the individual (shakhṣ) rather than the species, but the core idea remains.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Substantial change can occur gradually, like the heating of water.
Correction: Forms do not admit degrees; a thing cannot be partially of one form and partially of another. Therefore change of form must be instantaneous, unlike accidental changes.

Checkpoint
According to the argument, what happens to the species during the intermediate state if substantial change were gradual?
A) The species remains unchanged, so no change in the form occurs.
B) The species is destroyed, so the change is a motion.
C) The species becomes stronger, allowing gradual transition.
D) The species is replaced by another species gradually.

Key Takeaway
Substantial change (generation and corruption) is instantaneous because forms do not admit gradation; any gradual transition would require an impossible intermediate state.

Think of a core belief or habit you have tried to change gradually. Did you ever experience an 'intermediate state' where you felt you were neither fully committed nor fully free? According to Al-Jurjani's argument, that state either meant the old form still remained or it was already destroyed. Which was true for you, and how does that insight challenge your approach to change?
?

Are you perhaps avoiding the instantaneous decision to become something new? What would it look like to 'instantaneously' embrace a virtue you have been cultivating bit by bit?
?
The passage argues that change in essence is all at once, not piece by piece. Consider the moment you first truly understood a difficult truth about yourself or about faith. Was that understanding gradual, or did it strike you in an instant? How does this lesson affect your expectation of how God might transform the heart?

Proof that Substantial Change is Not Gradual Motion
Commentator argues that forms do not admit gradual change, so substantial change is generation and corruption.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why substantial change (generation and corruption) cannot be a gradual motion, and how the commentator defends this against the objection from qualitative motion.

Core Idea
Forms do not admit gradual change; substantial change is instantaneous generation or corruption.

In this passage, the commentator argues that the replacement of one substantial form by another cannot be a gradual process like motion. Instead, it must be instantaneous generation and corruption. He supports this by analyzing what would happen in an intermediate state and addresses an objection from motion in qualities. This lesson explores that argument and its defense.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's own argument come through before you interpret.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Understand the thesis
Identify the main claim: forms do not admit intensification (اشتداد) or diminution (تنقص), unlike qualities.
This sets up the argument that substantial change is not motion.
3
Analyze the intermediate state argument
The commentator considers what happens in the middle of a supposed gradual transition. If the type (نوع) of the substance remains, the change is only in concomitants, not the essence. If the type does not remain, the form is annihilated and a new one appears, which is not gradual.
This is the core logical proof.
4
Consider the succession of forms
The commentator then examines successive forms: if any form lasts an instant, motion stops; if all are momentary and succeed without interval, instants follow but not continuous motion; if there is a time gap, motion is discontinuous.
This reinforces the conclusion that the process is not gradual motion.
5
Examine the objection and response
An objection is raised from motion in quality (كيف). The response explains that the subject can remain without the specific qualities, so a temporal gap does not imply motion's impossibility.
The commentator clarifies that the first branch refers to change in concomitants, not essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الثالث : وقع الحركة من المقولات وتغيراتها المشاهدة ) لدلالتهما على أن العناصر ينقلب بعضها إلى بعض ( كما سيأتي ) في الموقف الرابع ( ثم نقول : ( في بيان أن تبديل الصورة بأخرى لا يجوز أن يكون تدريجياً فلا يكون حركة بل كوناً وفساداً ( الصور لا تقبل الاشتداد) بأن يتحرك محل الصورة إلى صورة أقوى منها ) ولا التنقص ) بأن يتحرك محلها إلى صورة أضعف منها على قياس الكيفيات التي تقع فيها الحركة، بل الصور لا تقبل الانتقال التدريجي مطلقاً بأن ينقل محل الصورة إلى صورة أخرى يسيراً يسيراً، سواء كانت الأخرى أقوى أو أضعف أو مساوية ) لأن في الوسط) أي في وسط الاشتداد أو التنقص، بل في وسط الانتقال التدريجي ( إن بقي نوعه ) أي نوع الجوهر المنتقل منه لم يكن التغير في الصورة أي لم يكن فيها اشتداد ولا تنقص بحسب ذاتها بل في لوازمها وصفاتها ولو قيل : إن بقي شخصه لكان أشمل وإن لم يبق نوعه أو شخصه كان ذلك عدم الصورة لا اشتدادها ولا تنقصها ولا الحركة فيها، إذ لا بد يحصل عقيبها صورة أخرى، فنقول : تلك الصورة المتعاقبة إن كان فيها ما آن واحد فقد سكنت الحركة في الصورة وإلا كانت كلها آنية الوجود، فإن تعاقبت بلا فصل تتالت الآنات وإن وجد فيما بين متعاقبين زمان خال عن تلك الصور الآنية كانت الحركة منقطعة، ونقض هذا الدليل بالحركة في الكيف وغيره من المقولات، وأجيب عنه بأن بقاء الموضوع بدون الكيفيات وسائر الأعراض جائز فلا يلزم من خلوه عن تلك الكيفيات الصورة الكلية وقبول صورة أخرى أشد منها . فعدم الصورة لا ينافي ذلك، وإن كان زوال صورة مكيفة بكيفية أشد يمنع أنه استحالة في التحقيق ولم يستقم قوله في الشق الأول لم يكن فيها اشتداد بحسب ذاتها بل في لوازمها، والجواب أن المراد هو الشق الأول ومنافاة عدم الصورة يظهر بملاحظة قوله إذ لا بد أن يحصل إلخ .
The Fourth Observatory - The Third Objective: The occurrence of motion among the categories and its changes. Observation: because they indicate that elements change into one another, as will come in the Fourth Stand. Then we say: (In explanation that the replacement of one form with another cannot be gradual, so it is not motion but generation and corruption.) Forms do not admit intensification—meaning that the locus of the form moves to a stronger form—nor diminution—meaning that its locus moves to a weaker form—analogous to the qualities in which motion occurs. Rather, forms absolutely do not admit gradual transition, meaning that the locus of the form moves to another form little by little, whether the other is stronger, weaker, or equal. Because in the intermediate state—i.e., in the middle of intensification or diminution, but rather in the middle of gradual transition—if its type remains (i.e., the type of the substance from which it moves), then the change is not in the form, i.e., there is no intensification or diminution in its essence, but rather in its concomitants and attributes. And if it were said: if its individual remains, it would be more comprehensive. And if its type or individual does not remain, then that is the annihilation of the form, not its intensification, diminution, or motion in it, because necessarily another form occurs afterward. So we say: those successive forms—if any of them is an instant, then motion in the form has ceased; otherwise they are all momentary in existence. If they succeed without interval, then instants follow one another. And if between two successors there is a time devoid of those momentary forms, then motion is discontinuous. This proof is challenged by motion in quality and other categories. And it is answered that the subsistence of the subject without the qualities and other accidents is permissible, so its being devoid of those qualities does not necessitate the universal form and acceptance of another stronger form. So the non-existence of the form does not contradict that. And if the removal of a form qualified by a stronger quality prevents that, it is an impossibility in reality. And his statement in the first branch—'there was no intensification in its essence but in its concomitants'—is not correct. The answer is that what is meant is the first branch, and the contradiction of the non-existence of the form appears by observing his statement: 'indeed, it must occur, etc.'
Source pages p. 225

Commentary
This passage is a proof that forms (صور) do not admit gradual change, so the replacement of one substantial form by another is not motion (حركة) but generation and corruption (كون وفساد). The commentator begins by stating the thesis: forms cannot undergo intensification (اشتداد) or diminution (تنقص), unlike qualities (كيفيات) which can. Then he argues that any supposed gradual transition would require an intermediate state. In that intermediate state, if the type (نوع) of the substance remains, then the change is not in the essence of the form but only in its concomitants (لوازم) and attributes. If the type or individual (شخص) does not remain, then the original form ceases to exist and a new one appears instantaneously, which is not gradual motion. The commentator then considers successive forms: if any form lasts an instant, motion stops; if all are momentary and succeed without temporal gap, instants follow, but that is not continuous motion; if there is a time gap, motion is discontinuous. The proof is then challenged by an objection from motion in quality (الحركة في الكيف). The answer is that the subject (موضوع) can remain without the specific qualities, so a temporal gap does not imply motion's impossibility. The commentary concludes by clarifying that the objection misunderstands the first branch; the intended meaning is that the form's change is in its concomitants, not its essence, and that the absence of a form does not conflict with gradual change in qualities.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that substantial change can be gradual like qualitative change.
Correction: The commentator shows that forms do not admit gradual intensification or diminution because the intermediate state would either leave the type unchanged (so no essential change) or eliminate the type (so the form is gone, not gradually changed).

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator argue that forms cannot undergo gradual change?
A) Because forms are eternal and unchanging.
B) Because if the type remains, the change is in concomitants, not the form; if the type does not remain, the form is annihilated.
C) Because motion requires a subject that persists, but forms do not.
D) Because forms are divisible and gradual change would require infinite division.

Key Takeaway
Substantial change is not gradual; it involves the instantaneous replacement of one form by another, which is generation and corruption, not motion.

Why Motion in Substantial Forms is Impossible: The Flowing Quality Argument
Proof that motion in substantial forms is inconceivable, using the analogy of accidental motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that motion in substantial forms is impossible because it would require an accidental flowing state, whereas substantial forms constitute the essence of matter.

Core Idea
Motion is accidental; substantial forms cannot change because matter’s existence depends on a specific form.

This passage lays out a key argument in Islamic philosophy against the possibility of motion in the category of substance. The author first shows that motion in accidents, like quality, involves a flowing state that is itself an accident. He then argues that substantial forms, which make matter a determinate existent, cannot undergo motion because matter would have to remain identical while its form changes—an impossibility. This lesson unpacks the logical steps of this argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the source passage carefully. Notice the author's style: he begins with an example from quality to establish a principle, then applies it to substance.
The passage is dense; take your time with each sentence.
2
Understand the flowing quality example
The author says that a moving quality (e.g., changing color) is a single 'flowing' state whose individuals change while the substrate (the object) remains. This state is an accident.
Contrast this with substantial form, which is not an accident.
3
Follow the application to substantial form
The argument then says that motion in substantial form would require the substrate (matter) to be actualized by a form throughout the motion. But matter alone has no existence; it exists only through a particular form. Therefore, matter cannot move from one form to another while remaining the same substrate.
This is the core of the proof: motion requires a continuous subject, but matter's identity changes with form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا محيص عن ذلك إلا ما أن المتحرك في الكيف مثلاً له فيما بين مبدأ حركته ومنتهاها كيفية واحدة سيالة كما عرفت ومثل هذا الحال السيال الذي يتبدل أفراده على محله مع بقاء المحل بشخصه لا بد أن يكون عرضاً لتقوم محله بدونه فلا يتصور حركة في الصور المقومة لمحالها ( وأيضاً فمبدأ الحركة) أي ما تقوم به الحركة وهو المتحرك (موجود) لا محالة في زمان كونه متحركاً ( والمادة وحدها لا وجود لها فإنَّ المادة لا تتحصل ذاتاً معينة موجودة إلا بالصورة المعينة فلا يمكن ) حركتها إلا إذا كانت من مبدأ حركتها إلى منتهاها متصورة بصورة معينة فيمتنع تتحرك في الصورة بالضرورة، وقد يقال : تحصل المادة بشخصها إنما يكون بصورة متعاقبة لا بصورة واحدة معينة، فلا يلزم امتناع حركتها، ويجاب بأنها مع إحدى تلك الصور ذات متحصلة ومع صورة أخرى ذات متحصلة مغايرة للذات الأولى وليس لشيء من تلك الذوات المتحصلة حركة وانتقال من حالة إلى حالة أخرى، فلا حركة أصلاً، وهذا الجواب كما ترى مبني على أنَّ الهيولى ليست إلا شيئاً بالقوة لا تتحصل موجودة إلا بصورة معينة لما ثبت عندهم من أن وحدتها وتعددها واتصالها وانفصالها تابعة للصورة، فلو كانت في ذاتها متحصلة بالفعل لما كانت كذلك وللبحث فيه مجال
And there is no escape from that except that the moving thing in quality, for example, has between the beginning of its motion and its end a single flowing quality (كيفية سيالة) as you have known. And the likes of this flowing state (حال سيال) whose individuals change upon its substrate while the substrate remains in its identity must be an accident (عرض) because its substrate subsists by it without it. So motion in the forms that constitute their substrates is inconceivable. (And also the principle of motion) i.e. that by which motion subsists, namely the moving thing (exists) necessarily in the time of its being moving. (And matter alone has no existence, for matter does not become a determinate existent essence except by a particular form, so) its motion is only possible if from the beginning of its motion to its end it is conceived with a particular form. Thus it is necessarily impossible for it to move in form. And it may be said: The matter's actualization in its identity is only by successive forms, not by one particular form, so the impossibility of its motion does not follow. And it is answered: With each of those forms it has an actualized essence, and with another form it has another actualized essence different from the first essence, and none of those actualized essences has motion and transition from one state to another. So there is no motion at all. And this answer, as you see, is based on the fact that hyle (الهيولى) is only something in potentiality, not actualized as existing except by a particular form, as has been established among them that its unity, multiplicity, continuity, and discontinuity are dependent on the form. For if it were in itself actualized in act, it would not be so. And there is room for investigation in this.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is a proof that motion does not occur in substantial forms. The argument proceeds in several steps. First, it establishes that motion in the category of quality involves a single flowing quality (كيفية سيالة) that is an accident because its substrate remains identical while its individuals change. This shows that motion in accidental forms is possible, but the focus is on substantial forms. The second step argues that motion in substantial forms is inconceivable because the moving thing (the subject of motion) must exist during motion, and matter (مادة) alone has no existence except with a particular form (صورة). Therefore, if matter were to move in form, it would have to be conceived with one form throughout the motion, which is impossible because matter at the beginning and end would have different essences. The objection claims that matter is actualized by successive forms, so it could move from one form to another. The answer refutes this by pointing out that with each form, matter has a distinct actualized essence, and no single essence undergoes motion. This answer relies on the hylomorphic principle that prime matter (هيولى) is pure potentiality and only becomes actual through a specific form. The unity and multiplicity of matter depend on form. The passage ends by noting that there is room for further investigation, indicating that not all philosophers accept this argument without qualification.

حال سيال
عرض
مادة
صورة

Checkpoint

A) Because matter cannot have a flowing state
B) Because matter has no existence without a particular form
C) Because all motion requires an accident
D) Because the substrate does not remain identical

Key Takeaway
Motion in substantial forms is impossible because matter has no independent existence; it is always actualized by a specific form, so any change would mean a new essence, not motion.

Reflect on one essential quality of your soul that you must protect at all costs, and commit to a specific action today to guard it.

Do you live as if you are self-sufficient, or do you acknowledge that your form is sustained by His command? Objection and Answer: Successive Forms and the Impossibility of Motion
Objection and answer regarding the possibility of motion in substantial forms via successive forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that matter can be actualized by successive forms and the reply that this still does not yield motion because each form gives a distinct essence.

Core Idea
Successive forms do not produce motion; they produce distinct essences with no continuous subject.

After presenting the proof against motion in substantial forms, the author anticipates an objection: perhaps matter is actualized by a series of forms, so its identity is preserved through the succession. The answer, based on the hylomorphic principle that prime matter is pure potentiality, shows that each form yields a different actualized essence, and no single essence undergoes motion. This lesson analyzes the objection and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
The objection says: matter gets its individual identity from successive forms, not one fixed form. So matter could move from one form to another.
This challenges the assumption that matter must have one form throughout motion.
2
Understand the answer's core point
The answer argues that with each form, matter has a different actualized essence. Motion requires one essence that changes; here we have multiple essences, so no motion.
The key is that the subject of motion must be one and the same thing undergoing change.
3
Note the underlying hylomorphic assumption
The answer relies on the idea that prime matter (هيولى) is pure potentiality and only becomes actual through form. Its unity and multiplicity depend on form.
If matter were already actual on its own, the argument might not hold—hence the final comment that there is room for investigation.

And the likes of this flowing state (حال سيال) whose individuals change upon its substrate while the substrate remains in its identity must be an accident (عرض) because its substrate subsists by it without it. Commentary
This passage is a proof that motion does not occur in substantial forms. The argument proceeds in several steps. First, it establishes that motion in the category of quality involves a single flowing quality (كيفية سيالة) that is an accident because its substrate remains identical while its individuals change. The objection claims that matter is actualized by successive forms, so it could move from one form to another. Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that motion in substantial forms is possible if matter can take on successive forms while remaining the same substrate.
Correction: The answer shows that each form creates a new actualized essence, so there is no continuous subject to undergo motion.

Checkpoint
Why does the answer reject the objection that matter can be actualized by successive forms?
A) Because successive forms are impossible
B) Because each form yields a different actualized essence, so no motion
C) Because matter cannot have more than one form
D) Because the objection contradicts the definition of motion

Key Takeaway
Successive forms do not produce motion in substance because each form gives matter a distinct essence, and motion requires a single continuous subject.

The Objection of Successive Forms and Two Interpretations
Commentary examining objection that matter is actualized by successive forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection against the motion argument based on successive forms and the two possible interpretations of how matter is actualized.

Core Idea
Matter may be actualized either by particular forms or by being a distinct type with each form.

The commentary examines an objection that matter is actualized by successive forms, similar to a house built with different pillars over time. It then considers two interpretations of the answer: one where matter is only actualized by particular forms, and another where each combination of matter and form constitutes a distinct type. Understanding these interpretations is crucial for analyzing the motion argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Start by reading the objection that matter is actualized by successive forms, like a house with successive pillars. Note the analogy.
This sets up the problem of how matter can have motion if forms change.
2
Identify the two interpretations
The commentary presents two ways to understand the answer: either matter is only actualized by particular forms, or matter with each form constitutes a distinct type. Notice the difference.
The second interpretation leads to a denial of motion.
3
Understand why motion is negated in the second interpretation
In the second interpretation, each type is already fully actualized, so no intermediate state exists. Motion requires that state between potentiality and actuality.
This is a key logical point in the argument.
4
Review the condition for motion
The passage states that if matter is actualized by successive forms, there must be an intermediate state if transition is gradual. This connects motion to the need for potentiality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( إنما يكون بصور متعاقبة كالبيت فإنه متحصل بدعائم متعاقبة لا بدعائم معينة. قوله : ( وهذا الجواب إلخ لو قرر الجواب أنَّ الهيولى ليس بمحصلة إلا بالصور المعينة، أما لو قرر بأنَّ الهيولى مع إحدى الصور نوع محصلة وبالصورة الأخرى نوع آخر وليس في شيء من تلك الأنواع حركة حال فإن الحركة متوسطة بين مخصوصة القوة مخصوصة الفعل وهاهنا إما مخصوصة القوة أو مخصوصة الفعل فلما بني على تلك المقدمة قال : الهيولى مع كونها متحصلة في نفسها بتوارد الصور لا بد لها من حالة متوسطة بينها إذا كان الانتقال تدريجياً . قوله : ( لما كانت كذلك أي لما كانت وحدتها وتعددها واتصالها وانفصالها تابعة للصورة لا يستلزم أن يكون متحصلة في نفسها فإنهم قالوا : إنها متحصلة في نفسها بتوارد الصور قوله: ( ومثل هذا الحال السيال الذي يتبدل أفراده إلخ) فيه بحث لأنَّ الأفراد المتبدلة هي الأفراد الفرضية لا الحقيقية وإلا يرد الترديد السابق كما تحققته هناك، فكما أنَّ المحل باق بالشخص لم يتغير ذاته كذلك الحال السيال باق بشخصه لم يتغير ذاته، فلو فرض كونه مقوماً لمحله لم يلزم انتفاء المحل. قوله : ( وللبحث فيه مجال ) إذ يجوز أن يقال : تلك للصورة إذا زالت عن الهيولى واتصلت بها صورة أخرى حصل مجموع غير المجموع الأول، ولكن الهيولى باقية على حالها فإن قلت : إذا كانت الهيولى تابعة للصورة في التشخص كان التشخص الحاصل باعتبار هذه الصورة غير التشخص الحاصل بسبب تلك الصورة . قلت : قد صرح ابن سينا بأن الوحدة الشخصية للمادة مستحفظة بالوحدة النوعية للصورة لا بالوحدة الشخصية لها فتبعية الهيولى للصورة النوعية لا للصورة الشخصية كما يشعر به قوله : لا تتحصل موجودة إلا بصورة معينة، ويدل عليه أيضاً إطباقهم على قدم الهيولى شخصاً مع حدوث الصور المتعاقبة بالشخص فحينئذ لا يتبدل هويتها بتبدل الصور لها فيجوز أن تتحرك في الصور الشخصية وهاهنا بحث آخر، وهو أن البيان المذكور على تقدير تمامه إنما يفيد عدم حركة الهيولى في الصور الجسمية ولا يفيد عدم حركة الجسم في الصور النوعية والشخصية، كما لم يفد عدم حركة الهيولى في الصورة الشخصية مع المدعى عدم الحركة في الجوهر مطلقاً .
His saying: (It is only by successive forms), like the house, for it is actualized by successive pillars, not by specific pillars. His saying: (And this answer, etc.) If the answer is established that hyle is not actualized except by particular forms, but if it is established that hyle with one form is a type actualized and with another form is another type, and none of those types has motion in a state, for motion is intermediate between specific potentiality and specific actuality, and here it is either specific potentiality or specific actuality, so when it is built on that premise, he said: Hyle, while being actualized in itself by the succession of forms, necessarily has an intermediate state between them if the transition is gradual. His saying: (When it is so), i.e., when its unity, multiplicity, continuity, and discontinuity are dependent on the form, it does not entail that it is actualized in itself, for they said: It is actualized in itself by the succession of forms. His saying: (And the likes of this flowing state whose individuals change, etc.) There is investigation in this because the changing individuals are hypothetical individuals, not real; otherwise the previous repetition would occur, as you have realized there. For just as the substrate remains in identity, its essence does not change, so also the flowing state remains in its identity, its essence does not change. If it were supposed to be constitutive of its substrate, the non-existence of the substrate would not follow. His saying: (And there is room for investigation in this) because it may be said: That form, when it departs from hyle and another form attaches to it, a whole other than the first whole results, but hyle remains in its state. If you say: If hyle is dependent on form in individuation, then the individuation obtained by this form is other than the individuation obtained by that form. I say: Ibn Sina has stated that the personal unity of matter is preserved by the specific unity of the form, not by its personal unity. So hyle's dependence is on the specific form, not on the personal form, as his saying indicates: It is not actualized as existing except by a particular form. This is also indicated by their agreement on the eternity of hyle in person with the temporal origination of successive forms in person. Therefore, its identity does not change with the change of forms, so it may move in personal forms. And here is another investigation: that the mentioned demonstration, assuming its completeness, only proves the non-motion of hyle in bodily forms, and does not prove the non-motion of the body in specific and personal forms, just as it did not prove the non-motion of hyle in personal form, despite the claim of non-motion in substance absolutely.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the previous argument, explaining and critiquing its details. It begins by discussing the objection that matter is actualized by successive forms, comparing it to a house built with successive pillars. The commentary then examines two possible interpretations of the answer: either hyle is not actualized except by particular forms, or hyle with each form constitutes a distinct type. In the latter case, no motion occurs because motion requires an intermediate state between specific potentiality and actuality. Next, it addresses the point about the flowing state, arguing that the changing individuals are hypothetical, not real, so the flowing state's identity remains unchanged. This means that even if the flowing state were constitutive of the substrate, the substrate would not cease to exist. The final part discusses Ibn Sina's view that matter's personal unity is preserved by the specific form, not the personal form. Thus, matter can move in personal forms because its essence does not change. The commentary concludes that the original argument only rules out motion of hyle in bodily forms, not motion of the body in specific or personal forms.

Key Terms
هيولى
صورة
حركة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what is required for motion to occur in matter?
A) Matter must be actualized by a single form
B) Matter must have an intermediate state between specific potentiality and actuality
C) Matter must change its specific form continuously
D) Matter must remain in a state of pure potentiality

Key Takeaway
The second interpretation of matter's actualization by forms negates motion because it leaves no room for an intermediate state between potentiality and actuality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you avoiding the messy intermediate process of growth in your life, preferring to stay either fully potential (waiting) or fully actual (completed)? What would it look like to embrace the gradual transition today?
?
The passage says motion requires an intermediate state. Where in your spiritual journey are you impatient for immediate results, refusing the steps in between? Commit to one area where you will accept the process.
?
Which interpretation mirrors your view of personal change—do you see yourself as the same person with different attributes, or a completely different person with each change? How does that view affect your sense of accountability before Allah for your past and future?

The Flowing State and Identity of the Substrate
Commentary on the flowing state and the identity of the substrate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentary defends the identity of the substrate and the flowing state against objections based on changing individuals.

Core Idea
Changing individuals in a flowing state are hypothetical, not real, so the substrate's identity remains unchanged.

The commentary continues by addressing the nature of the flowing state, where individuals seem to change. It argues that these changing individuals are only hypothetical, not real, and that both the substrate and the flowing state retain their identity. This clarifies why the substrate can persist even if the flowing state is constitutive.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the flowing state
Focus on the part where the commentary discusses 'the likes of this flowing state whose individuals change'. Note the mention of hypothetical individuals.
The commentary distinguishes between hypothetical and real individuals.
2
Understand the distinction between hypothetical and real
The changing individuals are hypothetical, not real. Otherwise, a previous repetition would occur. Recognize this as a key defense of identity.
This is a philosophical move to preserve continuity.
3
See how the substrate and flowing state retain identity
Just as the substrate remains in its identity, so does the flowing state. Even if the flowing state were constitutive, it does not cause the substrate to cease.
4
Connect to the overall argument
This supports the idea that matter's identity persists despite changes in forms, which is relevant to the motion argument.

Ibn Sina's View on Personal Unity and the Argument's Limitation
Commentary on Ibn Sina's view and the limitation of the motion argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's distinction between personal and specific unity of matter and why the motion argument only rules out motion in bodily forms.

Core Idea
Matter's personal unity is preserved by the specific form, not the personal form, allowing motion in personal forms.

The commentary introduces Ibn Sina's view that matter's personal unity is maintained by the specific form, not the personal form. This allows matter to move in personal forms even as its identity persists. The lesson also reveals a critical limitation: the original argument only disproves motion of hyle in bodily forms, not motion of the body in specific or personal forms, contrary to the claim of absolute non-motion in substance.

Guided Path
1
Read Ibn Sina's view on personal unity
Focus on the part where Ibn Sina is quoted: the personal unity of matter is preserved by the specific unity of the form, not by its personal unity.
This distinguishes between the type of form and the individual form.
2
Understand the implication for motion
Because matter's identity depends on the specific form, not the personal form, it can remain the same while acquiring different personal forms. This allows motion in personal forms.
3
Identify the limitation of the original argument
The original argument only proves that hyle does not move in bodily forms; it does not prove that the body does not move in specific or personal forms.
This is a significant concession.
4
Consider the broader claim
The comment concludes that the argument does not establish absolute non-motion in substance, only a partial restriction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

I say: Ibn Sina has stated that the personal unity of matter is preserved by the specific unity of the form, not by its personal unity. So hyle's dependence is on the specific form, not on the personal form, as his saying indicates: It is not actualized as existing except by a particular form. This is also indicated by their agreement on the eternity of hyle in person with the temporal origination of successive forms in person. Therefore, its identity does not change with the change of forms, so it may move in personal forms. And here is another investigation: that the mentioned demonstration, assuming its completeness, only proves the non-motion of hyle in bodily forms, and does not prove the non-motion of the body in specific and personal forms, just as it did not prove the non-motion of hyle in personal form, despite the claim of non-motion in substance absolutely.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the previous argument, explaining and critiquing its details. It begins by discussing the objection that matter is actualized by successive forms, comparing it to a house built with successive pillars. The commentary then examines two possible interpretations of the answer: either hyle is not actualized except by particular forms, or hyle with each form constitutes a distinct type. In the latter case, no motion occurs because motion requires an intermediate state between specific potentiality and actuality. Next, it addresses the point about the flowing state, arguing that the changing individuals are hypothetical, not real, so the flowing state's identity remains unchanged. This means that even if the flowing state were constitutive of the substrate, the substrate would not cease to exist. The final part discusses Ibn Sina's view that matter's personal unity is preserved by the specific form, not the personal form. Thus, matter can move in personal forms because its essence does not change. The commentary concludes that the original argument only rules out motion of hyle in bodily forms, not motion of the body in specific or personal forms.

Key Terms
وحدة شخصية
وحدة نوعية
ابن سينا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think the original motion argument proves that no motion occurs in any substance at all.
Correction: The commentary shows it only rules out motion of hyle in bodily forms, leaving room for motion in specific and personal forms.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what does the original motion argument actually prove?
A) Non-motion of all substances
B) Non-motion of hyle in bodily forms
C) Non-motion of the body in specific forms
D) Non-motion of matter in personal forms

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's distinction allows matter to move in personal forms, and the original argument only rules out motion in bodily forms, not all motion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn Sina says matter's personal unity is preserved by the specific form, not the personal form. What are the 'personal forms' in your life—roles, abilities, relationships—that you think define you? If one of them changed or disappeared, would you still know who you are? What unchanging specific form (your fitrah, your soul) do you rely on?
?
The commentator notes that the original argument only covers bodily forms, not all motion. Are there areas in your faith or worldview where you have absolutized a partial truth, assuming it covers everything? Where do you need to humbly admit the limits of your understanding and remain open to deeper investigation?
?
Ibn Sina's view allowed matter to move in personal forms. Where in your spiritual life have you refused to change because you feared losing your identity? What change is God calling you to make that would actually preserve your core but transform your personal forms? Will you take a concrete step toward that change today?

Relation is Not an Independent Nature
Sanusi explains the non-independence of relation and illustrates motion in relation through the example of water temperature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why motion in the category of relation is always dependent on motion in its substrate.

Core Idea
Relation has no independent existence; it follows its substrate.

This lesson begins by establishing the foundational principle that the category of relation (المضاف) is not an independent nature but rather depends on something else. The passage explains that any change in relation must be traced back to a change in its substrate, and provides a concrete example involving water temperature.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the English translation. Notice the structure: a claim, a rule, and an example.
The passage is dense; focus on the logical flow.
2
Identify the key claim about non-independence
The passage states that relation is not independent in concept. Find the sentence that expresses this and the reasoning that follows.
The claim is at the beginning: 'وأما المضاف فطبيعة غير مستقلة'
3
Trace the example of water temperature
The example shows how motion in relation (being hotter or colder) follows motion in quality (temperature). Understand why the relation changes gradually as the water's quality changes.
The example illustrates the dependence of relation on its substrate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وأما المضاف فطبيعة غير مستقلة ) بنفسها في المفهومة ( بل) هي ( تابعة لغيرها) قوله : ( فإن كان متبوعها قابلاً للأشد والأضعف قبلهما المضاف أيضاً وإلا فلا) يعني الإضافة تابعة لمعروضها في الحركة بل في التغير مطلقاً لأنها لو تغيرت بلا تغير في معروضها لكانت مستقلة بالمفهومية وعلى هذا فإن كانت الإضافة عارضة لإحدى المقولات الأربع وقعت الحركة فيها تبعاً لها كما إذا فرض أن ماء أشد سخونة من ماء آخر وتحرك في الكيف حتى صار سخونته أضعف من سخونة الآخر، فإن هذا الماء قد انتقل من نوع من الإضافة أعني الأشدية إلى نوع آخر منها أعني الأضعفية انتقالاً تدريجياً، فقد تحرك الجسم في الإضافة تبعا لحركته في معروضها الحقيقي أعني الكيف. قوله : ( غير مستقلة بالمفهومية) أي لا يعقل إلا عارضة لمقولة أخرى، فالعروض المقولة أخرى معتبر في ذاتها فلا يعرض لها حكم من الأحكام بالذات فضلاً عن الحركة لأن أقل درجات المعروض أن يكون مستقلاً بتلك المعروضية فارتفع النقض والمنع قوله الآتي في الشفاء أن التضاد لا يعرض الإضافة فإن الإضافات طبائع مستقلة بأنفسها فيمتنع أن يعرض لها التضاد، لأن أقل درجات المعروض أن يكون مستقلاً بتلك المعروضية فأما كون الآخر ضداً لا براد كالحار والبارد فلأن الإضافات لما كانت طبيعة غير مستقلة بل تابعة لمعروضها وجب أن تكون في الحكم أيضاً تابعة، وإلا لكانت مستقلة فيه هذا لكنه في طبيعياته، وأما مقولة المضاف فبينه أن يكون الانتقال فيها أي هو من حال إلى حال دفعة وإن اختلف في بعض المواضع فيكون التغيير في الحقيقة واقعاً في مقولة أخرى عرضت لها الإضافة، والإضافة من شأنها أن تلحق مقولات أخرى ولا يتحقق بذاتها فإذا كانت المقولة مما يقبل الأشد والأضعف عرض للإضافة مثل ذلك فبين كلاميه تدافع فإن الأول أن لا يقع الانتقال مطلقاً في الإضافة إلا تبعاً لمقولة أخرى والثاني يدل على الانتقال، ولو نفي فيها بالذات يعدم كونها عارضة للموضوع بتوسط مقولة أخرى وإذا كانت عارضة بتوسط مقولة أخرى كانت الانتقال فيها بتبع تلك المقولة، فالحكم بأنها غير مستقلة حكم أكثري لأن عروضها بتوسط المقولات أكثر.
(As for the relative (المضاف), it is a nature not independent) in itself in concept, (rather) it is (dependent on something else). His saying: (If its dependent is receptive to intensity and weakness, then the relative also accepts them, otherwise not) means that relation (الإضافة) follows its substrate in motion, indeed in change absolutely, because if it changed without a change in its substrate, it would be independent in concept. And on this, if relation is accidental to one of the four categories, motion occurs in it dependently, as when it is supposed that water is hotter than another water and moves in quality until its heat becomes weaker than the heat of the other, for this water has transferred from one type of relation, namely intensity, to another type, namely weakness, gradually. So the body moves in relation following its motion in its true substrate, namely quality. His saying: (Not independent in concept) means it is only conceived as accidental to another category, so its being accidental to another category is considered in its essence. Therefore, no ruling applies to it in itself, let alone motion, because the least degree of a substrate is that it be independent in that substrate-hood. So the objection and prevention are removed. His upcoming saying in the Shifa that opposition does not occur to relation, for relations are independent natures in themselves so opposition cannot occur to them, because the least degree of a substrate is that it be independent in that substrate-hood. As for the other being a contrary, not intended like hot and cold, this is because relations, since they are a nature not independent but dependent on their substrate, must also be dependent in judgment, otherwise they would be independent in it. But this is in his natural philosophy. As for the category of relation, it is clear that transition in it is from state to state instantaneously, although it differs in some places. So the change in reality occurs in another category to which the relation attaches. And relation's property is that it attaches to other categories and is not actualized by itself. So if the category accepts intensity and weakness, relation also accepts the like. So there is a conflict between his two statements: the first is that transition in relation occurs not absolutely except dependently on another category, and the second indicates transition. And if it were negated in itself, it would nullify its being accidental to the subject through another category. And if it is accidental through another category, transition in it is dependent on that category. So the judgment that it is not independent is a predominant judgment because its accidentally occurring through categories is more frequent.
Source pages p. 228

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of whether motion can occur in the category of relation (المضاف). It begins with the claim that relation is not an independent nature; it is conceptually dependent on something else. The commentary explains that if the substrate (the thing to which relation attaches) admits intensity and weakness, then the relation also admits them, but if not, then not. This is because relation follows its substrate in motion and change. If relation could change without a change in its substrate, it would be independent. The example of water becoming hotter or colder illustrates this: the relation of being hotter or weaker changes as the water's quality changes, so motion in relation is dependent on motion in quality. The commentary then addresses a potential objection from the Shifa (Ibn Sina) that opposition does not occur in relation because relations are independent natures. It resolves this by noting that relation is not independent in concept and must follow its substrate in judgment. The passage then notes a conflict in Ibn Sina's statements: one suggests relation only changes dependently, another indicates instantaneous transition. The resolution is that change in relation is real but occurs in the substrate category, and relation is not self-subsistent. Ultimately, the judgment of non-independence is prevalent, as relation usually occurs through other categories.

Key Terms
مضاف
إضافة
معروض
أشد وأضعف
كيف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the category of relation can change independently of its substrate.
Correction: The passage shows that relation is not independent; it follows its substrate in motion. Any change in relation must be traced to a change in the substrate.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does motion occur in the category of relation?
A) Whenever the relation itself changes
B) When the substrate changes in a way that affects the relation
C) Only in the category of quality
D) Only in instantaneous transitions

Key Takeaway
Motion in the category of relation is always dependent on change in its substrate; relation has no independent existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your relationship with Allah: have you been treating it as something that can change without a change in your heart? How does this lesson correct that, and what will you do today to align your inner quality with the relation you claim?
?
Think of a relationship with a person you consider 'stronger' or 'weaker' than yourself. Have you ever assumed that relation can change while your own character remains unchanged? What does this lesson demand you examine about yourself?
?
The water example shows relation follows quality. What is the 'quality' in your soul that your relationship with Allah follows – is it sincerity, love, or heedlessness? Are you willing to let that quality change to improve the relation, even if it requires painful transformation?

Resolving the Objection on Relation’s Non-Independence
Sanusi clarifies the concept of non-independence, addresses the Shifa objection, and resolves the conflict by explaining the predominant nature of relation's dependence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentary resolves the apparent conflict between the non-independence of relation and Ibn Sina's statement about opposition in relation.

Core Idea
Relation's non-independence is a predominant judgment, not absolute.

This lesson continues the discussion by addressing an objection from Ibn Sina's Shifa regarding opposition in relation. The passage explains that relation's dependence on its substrate means it cannot have independent judgments, and any apparent instantaneous transition actually occurs in another category, concluding that the non-independence of relation is a predominant (akthari) judgment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. This section is more argumentative, involving an objection and its resolution.
Pay attention to the shift from explanation to objection.
2
Identify the objection from Shifa
Find the part where Ibn Sina's view is cited: that relations are independent natures and thus cannot have opposition. Understand why this seems to conflict with the earlier claim.
The objection starts with 'قوله الآتي في الشفاء أن التضاد لا يعرض الإضافة'
3
Understand the resolution
The passage resolves the objection by reiterating that relation is not independent and must follow its substrate in judgment. It then notes a conflict in Ibn Sina's own statements and explains that the non-independence of relation is a predominant (akthari) judgment.
The resolution shows that the apparent contradiction is reconciled by the concept of predominance.

The Instantaneous Transition in 'When'
Presents the objection and Ibn Sina's claim of instantaneous transition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that transition in the category of 'when' is instantaneous due to the continuity of time and the role of the 'now'.

Core Idea
Time's continuity makes transition in 'when' instantaneous.

In this lesson, we examine the initial claim that changes in the category of 'when' occur instantaneously. The passage presents an objection that 'when' might be intrinsic to time, and then Ibn Sina's argument for instantaneity using the concept of the 'now' as a boundary between times.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage that presents the objection and Ibn Sina's claim of instantaneous transition. Notice the key terms: زمان (time), آن (now), دفعة (instant).
The passage is from Ibn Sina's al-Shifa.
2
Understand the objection
The objection suggests that 'when' (متى) could be intrinsic to time, like priority and posteriority. This challenges the idea that 'when' changes only by reference to another time.
3
Analyze the instantaneity argument
Ibn Sina argues that because time's parts are continuous and share the 'now' as a common boundary, transition from one time to another is instantaneous. Identify the key steps in his reasoning.
Focus on the role of the 'now' as the end of one time and beginning of another.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الرابع - المقصد الثالث : وقع الحركة من المقولات وتغيراتها
زمان واعترض بأنه يجوز أن يكون عروض متى للزمان لذاته لا لزمان آخر كعروض القبلية والبعدية (و) قال (في الشفاء : يشبه أن يكون الانتقال في متى دفعياً إذ الانتقال من سنة إلى سنة ومن شهر إلى شهر يكون دفعة وذلك لأن أجزاء الزمان متصل بعضها ببعض والفصل المشترك بينها هو الآن، فإذا فرض زمانان يشتركان في آن فقبل ذلك الآن يستمر للموضوع متاه بالقياس إلى الزمان الأول وبعده يستمر متاه بالقياس إلى الزمان الثاني، وذلك الآن نهاية وجود الأول وبداية حصول الثاني فلا له تدريج في الانتقال ويرد عليه أن الفاصل بين أجزاء المسافة حدود غير منقسمة فيكون الانتقال من بعض تلك الأجزاء إلى بعض دفعياً أيضاً، ولكن إذا فرض مكانان بينهما مسافة منقسمة كان الانتقال من أحدهما إلى الآخر تدريجياً فكذا الحال في الانتقال من زمان إلى زمان آخر بينهما زمان كالفجر والمغرب مثلاً، فإنه يكون تدريجياً أيضاً لا دفعياً، ثم قال في الشفاء : ويشبه أن يكون حال متى كحال الإضافة أن الانتقال فيه يكون تبعاً للانتقال في شيء آخر من كم أو كيف فيقع التغير في ذلك الشيء أولاً، ويكون الزمان لازماً لذلك التغير فيعرض بسببه فيه التبدل، وإليه أشار بقوله : (وهو) أي متى ( كالإضافة ) في قبول الحركة على سبيل التبعية ( لأنه نسبة تابعة لمعروضها ) فلا يستقل بالمفهومية والتبدل وقد عرفت ما فيه ( وكذا الملك ) فإنه أيضاً مقولة نسبية تابعة لمعروضها في التبدل والاستقرار ( وأما ) مقولتا (أن قوله : (ثم قال في الشفاء : يشبه أن يكون إلخ ) يريد أن الانتقال الحاصل في متى بطريقين أحدهما أن يكون بسبب انتقال الزمان وعدم استقراره، وذلك دفعي لأن الزمان متصل واحد في اندفع الإيراد المذكور بقوله : ويرد عليه إلخ، وهو ظاهر وثانيهما الانتقال الحاصل بسبب تغير الأمور الواقعة فيه، وهذا الانتقال لما وقع فيه التغيران كان التغير فيه آنياً، فالانتقال من متى آني وإن كان تدريجياً فتدريجي، فملخص كلام الشيخ في النجاة أنه لا يقع الحركة في متى بالذات، وفي الشفاء أن الانتقال الحاصل فيه بسبب تغير الزمان وعدم استقراره دفعي والانتقال الحاصل فيه بسبب تغير الأمور الواقعة فيه التغير في شيء فلا يكون مستقلاً بالحركة. قوله : ( وقد عرفت ما فيه قد عرفت اندفاعه بما حررناه . قوله : ( وكذا الملك فإنه إلخ) هذا البيان غير تام لورود النقض بالمنع المذكور سابقاً، وفي
The Fourth Observatory - Third Objective: The occurrence of motion among the categories and their changes. Time (zaman) and it was objected that it is possible that the occurrence of when (mata) to time (zaman) is by itself, not by another time, like the occurrence of priority and posteriority. And he said in Al-Shifa: It seems that the transition in 'when' is instantaneous (duf'iyan), since the transition from year to year and from month to month occurs instantaneously, because the parts of time are continuous with each other and the common dividing point between them is the now (al-an). So if two times are assumed sharing a now, then before that now the subject continues with its 'when' relative to the first time, and after it continues with its 'when' relative to the second time, and that now is the end of the existence of the first and the beginning of the occurrence of the second, so there is no gradualness (tadrij) in the transition. And it is objected that the separating line between parts of distance are indivisible limits, so the transition from some of those parts to others is also instantaneous, but if two places are assumed with a divisible distance between them, the transition from one to the other is gradual; so likewise the case in transition from one time to another time between which there is a time, like dawn and sunset for example, then it is also gradual, not instantaneous. Then he said in Al-Shifa: It seems that the case of 'when' is like the case of relation (al-idafa), that the transition in it is secondary to the transition in something else among quantity (kam) or quality (kayf); so the change occurs in that thing first, and time is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of that change, and so due to it the change occurs in it. And to this he pointed by his saying: (It) i.e. 'when' (is like relation) in accepting motion in a secondary way, (because it is a relation dependent on its subject) so it is not independent (yastaqill) in concept (al-mafhumiyya) and change, and you have known what is in it. (And likewise possession (al-milk)) for it also is a relative category dependent on its subject in change and stability. (As for) the two categories (that... ) His statement: (Then he said in Al-Shifa: It seems that is (ilkh)) He intends that the transition occurring in 'when' is in two ways: one of them is due to the transition of time itself and its lack of stability, and that is instantaneous because time is one continuous in itself and its 'when' in this respect is one with no multiplicity let alone transition; and if a division is assumed, the common boundary between the two times is the now, so the transition occurring in the things that occur due to the transition of time and its lack of stability is instantaneous. And by this the aforementioned objection is repelled by his saying: And it is objected to it (ilkh) , and it is clear. And the second way is the transition occurring due to the change of the things occurring in it, and this transition since it involves two changes, the change in it is momentary; so the transition from 'when' is momentary, and if it is gradual, then it is gradual. So the summary of the shaykh's speech in Al-Najat is that motion does not occur in 'when' essentially (bi al-dhat), and in Al-Shifa that the transition occurring in it due to the change of time and its lack of stability is instantaneous, and the transition occurring in it due to the change of the things occurring in it is secondary to what is in the change, that there is no confusion from the shaykh's speech, and that he is not undecided about the occurrence of motion in 'when' as the wording of the text (al-matan) might suggest and as some deficient people believed. His statement: (So it is not independent in concept) meaning that the occurrence of something is not conceived except after considering the change in something, so it is not independent in motion. His statement: (And you have known what is in it; you have known its repulsion by what we have edited. His statement: (And likewise possession (al-milk) that is (ilkh)) This explanation is not complete because of a counterexample by the prevention mentioned earlier, and in (wa fi)...
Source pages p. 231

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on whether motion occurs in the category of 'when' (متى) and 'possession' (الملك). It begins with an objection: perhaps the occurrence of 'when' to time is intrinsic and not dependent on another time, like priority and posteriority. The author then quotes Ibn Sīnā's al-Shifā', which suggests that transition in 'when' is instantaneous because time's parts are continuous, sharing the 'now' as a common boundary. However, a counter-objection is raised: if there is an interval between two times (like between dawn and sunset), the transition is gradual, not instantaneous. Ibn Sīnā then compares 'when' to 'relation' (الإضافة): motion in it is secondary, following a change in another category (quantity or quality). The commentary explains that transition in 'when' occurs in two ways: (1) due to the flux of time itself, which is instantaneous (because time is a continuum and its own 'when' is singular), and (2) due to changes in the things occurring in time, which may be gradual. Thus, motion in 'when' is not essential but secondary. The passage also notes that 'possession' (الملك) is similarly a relative category. The author concludes that Ibn Sīnā's position is unambiguous: motion does not occur in 'when' essentially, and the apparent confusion arises from misunderstanding the text.

Key Terms
متى
الزمان
الآن
دفعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that motion in 'when' is always gradual because time flows gradually.
Correction: Ibn Sina argues that due to the continuous nature of time and the role of the 'now', the transition from one time to another is instantaneous, not gradual.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is the transition in 'when' from year to year instantaneous?
A) Because years are indivisible units
B) Because time's parts are continuous and share the 'now' as a boundary
C) Because the subject does not change
D) Because motion in 'when' is impossible

Key Takeaway
Transition in the category of 'when' can be instantaneous due to time's continuity and the 'now' acting as a boundary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the 'now' as the boundary between your past and future, are you aware that your spiritual state can change in an instant? Is there a specific 'now' in your life where you are avoiding an instantaneous decision that could transform your relationship with Allah?
?
Ibn Sina argues that transition in 'when' can be instantaneous because time's continuity hides the abrupt shift. In your own life, do you use the seeming continuity of time to delay repentance, thinking gradual change is sufficient? What would it mean for you to treat the next 'now' as the moment of decisive spiritual transition?
?
The 'now' is both an end and a beginning. If you knew that your current 'now' were the end of your time in this world, would you be ready to face its beginning in the next? What one thing would you immediately change to make that transition a success?

The Counter-Objection and Gradual Transition
Presents the counter-objection using spatial analogy to argue for gradual transition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the counter-objection that transition in 'when' can be gradual when there is an interval between times.

Core Idea
When times are separated by an interval, transition in 'when' becomes gradual.

After the claim of instantaneity, a counter-objection arises: just as spatial transitions can be gradual when there is distance, temporal transitions can be gradual when there is an interval between two times. This lesson explores that objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the counter-objection
Read the Arabic passage that presents the objection using the analogy of spatial distance. Identify the key analogy: places with divisible distance lead to gradual transition.
2
Compare spatial and temporal transitions
The objection parallels how spatial motion from one place to another is gradual if the intervening distance is divisible. Similarly, transition from dawn to sunset is gradual because there is time between them.
3
Understand the consequence
This objection shows that transition in 'when' is not always instantaneous; it can be gradual when the two times are not adjacent.
This prepares for the resolution in the next lesson.

?

?

Motion in 'When' as Secondary
Presents Ibn Sina's comparison of 'when' to relation and the two-way resolution, plus the extension to possession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the resolution that motion in 'when' is secondary, either following time's own flux (instantaneous) or changes in the subject (gradual), and that 'possession' shares this secondary nature.

Core Idea
Motion in 'when' and 'possession' is secondary, dependent on changes in other categories.

Ibn Sina resolves the debate by comparing 'when' to relation (الإضافة): motion in it is not essential but follows changes elsewhere. This lesson covers that comparison, the two ways of transition, and the similar status of 'possession' (الملك).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on relation
Read the Arabic passage where Ibn Sina likens 'when' to relation. Notice the claim that motion in it is secondary (تابع) and that it is not independent in concept.
2
Understand the two ways
The commentary explains two ways: (1) transition due to time's own flux (instantaneous), and (2) transition due to changes in the subject (gradual).
This reconciles the instantaneous and gradual views.
3
Apply to possession
The same reasoning applies to the category of 'possession' (الملك), which is also a relative category.
